Lady Audley 's Secret

 BRADDON 



 LADY AUDLEY 's SECRET . 

 by Miss M.E. Braddon . 

 CHAPTER I. 

 LUCY . 

 it lie down in a hollow , rich with fine old timber and luxuriant pasture ; and you come upon it through an avenue of lime , border on either side by meadow , over the high hedge of which the cattle look inquisitively at you as you pass , wonder , perhaps , what you want ; for there be no thorough - fare , and unless you be go to the Court you have no business there at all . 

 at the end of this avenue there be an old arch and a clock tower , with a stupid , bewildering clock , which have only one hand and which jump straight from one hour to the next and be therefore always in extreme . through this arch you walk straight into the garden of Audley Court . 

 a smooth lawn lie before you , dot with group of rhododendron , which grow in more perfection here than anywhere else in the county . to the right there be the kitchen garden , the fish - pond , and an orchard border by a dry moat , and a broken ruin of a wall , in some place thick than it be high , and everywhere overgrown with trail ivy , yellow stonecrop , and dark moss . to the left there be a broad graveled walk , down which , year ago , when the place have be a convent , the quiet nun have walk hand in hand ; a wall border with espalier , and shadow on one side by goodly oak , which shut out the flat landscape , and circle in the house and garden with a darkening shelter . 

 the house face the arch , and occupy three side of a quadrangle . it be very old , and very irregular and rambling . the window be uneven ; some small , some large , some with heavy stone mullion and rich stain glass ; other with frail lattice that rattle in every breeze ; other so modern that they might have be add only yesterday . great pile of chimney rise up here and there behind the pointed gable , and seem as if they be so broken down by age and long service that they must have fall but for the straggling ivy which , crawl up the wall and trail even over the roof , wound itself about they and support they . the principal door be squeeze into a corner of a turret at one angle of the building , as if it be in hiding from dangerous visitor , and wish to keep itself a secret a noble door for all that old oak , and stud with great square - head iron nail , and so thick that the sharp iron knocker strike upon it with a muffled sound , and the visitor ring a clanging bell that dangle in a corner among the ivy , lest the noise of the knocking should never penetrate the stronghold . 

 a glorious old place . a place that visitor fall in rapture with ; feel a yearn wish to have do with life , and to stay there forever , star into the cool fish - pond and count the bubble as the roach and carp rise to the surface of the water . a spot in which peace seem to have take up her abode , set her soothe hand on every tree and flower , on the still pond and quiet alley , the shady corner of the old - fashioned room , the deep window - seat behind the paint glass , the low meadow and the stately avenue ay , even upon the stagnant well , which , cool and shelter as all else in the old place , hide itself away in a shrubbery behind the garden , with an idle handle that be never turn and a lazy rope so rotten that the pail have break away from it , and have fall into the water . 

 a noble place ; inside as well as out , a noble place a house in which you incontinently lose yourself if ever you be so rash as to attempt to penetrate its mystery alone ; a house in which no one room have any sympathy with another , every chamber run off at a tangent into an inner chamber , and through that down some narrow staircase lead to a door which , in its turn , lead back into that very part of the house from which you think yourself the furth ; a house that could never have be plan by any mortal architect , but must have be the handiwork of that good old builder , Time , who , add a room one year , and knock down a room another year , topple down a chimney coeval with the Plantagenets , and set up one in the style of the Tudors ; shake down a bit of Saxon wall , allow a Norman arch to stand here ; throw in a row of high narrow window in the reign of Queen Anne , and join on a dining - room after the fashion of the time of Hanoverian George I , to a refectory that have be stand since the Conquest , have contrive , in some eleven century , to run up such a mansion as be not elsewhere to be meet with throughout the county of Essex . of course , in such a house there be secret chamber ; the little daughter of the present owner , Sir Michael Audley , have fall by accident upon the discovery of one . a board have rattle under her foot in the great nursery where she play , and on attention be draw to it , it be find to be loose , and so removed , reveal a ladder , lead to a hiding - place between the floor of the nursery and the ceiling of the room below a hiding - place so small that he who have hide there must have crouch on his hand and knee or lain at full length , and yet large enough to contain a quaint old carve oak chest , half fill with priest ' vestment , which have be hide away , no doubt , in those cruel day when the life of a man be in danger if he be discover to have harbor a roman catholic priest , or to have mass say in his house . 

 the broad outer moat be dry and grass - grow , and the laden tree of the orchard hang over it with gnarl , straggle branch that draw fantastical shadow upon the green slope . within this moat there be , as I have say , the fish - pond a sheet of water that extend the whole length of the garden and border which there be an avenue call the lime - tree walk ; an avenue so shade from the sun and sky , so screen from observation by the thick shelter of the over - arch tree that it seem a choose place for secret meeting or for steal interview ; a place in which a conspiracy might have be plan , or a lover 's vow register with equal safety ; and yet it be scarcely twenty pace from the house . 

 at the end of this dark arcade there be the shrubbery , where , half bury among the tangle branch and the neglect weed , stand the rusty wheel of that old well of which I have speak . it have be of good service in its time , no doubt ; and busy nun have perhaps draw the cool water with their own fair hand ; but it have fall into disuse now , and scarcely any one at Audley Court know whether the spring have dry up or not . but shelter as be the solitude of this lime - tree walk , I doubt very much if it be ever put to any romantic us . often in the cool of the evening Sir Michael Audley would stroll up and down smoke his cigar , with his dog at his heel , and his pretty young wife dawdle by his side ; but in about ten minute the baronet and his companion would grow tired of the rustle lime and the still water , hide under the spread leaf of the water - lily , and the long green vista with the broken well at the end , and would stroll back to the drawing - room , where my lady play dreamy melody by Beethoven and Mendelssohn till her husband fall asleep in his easy - chair . 

 Sir Michael Audley be fifty - six year of age , and he have marry a second wife three month after his fifty - fifth birthday . he be a big man , tall and stout , with a deep , sonorous voice , handsome black eye , and a white beard a white beard which make he look venerable against his will , for he be as active as a boy , and one of the hard rider in the country . for seventeen year he have be a widower with an only child , a daughter , Alicia Audley , now eighteen , and by no mean too well pleased at have a step - mother bring home to the Court ; for Miss Alicia have reign supreme in her father 's house since her early childhood , and have carry the key , and jingle they in the pocket of her silk apron , and lose they in the shrubbery , and drop they into the pond , and give all manner of trouble about they from the hour in which she enter her teen , and have , on that account , delude herself into the sincere belief , that for the whole of that period , she have be keep the house . 

 but Miss Alicia 's day be over ; and now , when she ask anything of the housekeeper , the housekeeper would tell she that she would speak to my lady , or she would consult my lady , and if my lady please it should be do . so the baronet 's daughter , who be an excellent horsewoman and a very clever artist , spend most of her time out of door , rid about the green lane , and sketch the cottage child , and the plow - boy , and the cattle , and all manner of animal life that come in her way . she set her face with a sulky determination against any intimacy between herself and the baronet 's young wife ; and amiable as that lady be , she find it quite impossible to overcome Miss Alicia 's prejudice and dislike ; or to convince the spoilt girl that she have not do she a cruel injury by marry Sir Michael Audley . the truth be that Lady Audley have , in become the wife of Sir Michael , make one of those apparently advantageous match which be apt to draw upon a woman the envy and hatred of her sex . she have come into the neighborhood as a governess in the family of a surgeon in the village near Audley Court . no one know anything of she , except that she come in answer to an advertisement which Mr. Dawson , the surgeon , have insert in the Times . she come from London ; and the only reference she give be to a lady at a school at Brompton , where she have once be a teacher . but this reference be so satisfactory that none other be need , and Miss Lucy Graham be receive by the surgeon as the instructress of his daughter . her accomplishment be so brilliant and numerous , that it seem strange that she should have answer an advertisement offer such very moderate term of remuneration as those name by Mr. Dawson ; but Miss Graham seem perfectly well satisfied with her situation , and she teach the girl to play sonata by Beethoven , and to paint from nature after Creswick , and walk through a dull , out - of - the - way village to the humble little church , three time every Sunday , as contentedly as if she have no high aspiration in the world than to do so all the rest of her life . 

 People who observe this , account for it by say that it be a part of her amiable and gentle nature always to be light - hearte , happy and content under any circumstance . 

 wherever she go she seem to take joy and brightness with she . in the cottage of the poor her fair face shine like a sunbeam . she would sit for a quarter of an hour talk to some old woman , and apparently as pleased with the admiration of a toothless crone as if she have be listen to the compliment of a marquis ; and when she trip away , leave nothing behind she   for her poor salary give no scope to her benevolence , the old woman would burst out into senile rapture with her grace , beauty , and her kindliness , such as she never bestow upon the vicar 's wife , who half feed and clothe she . for you see , Miss Lucy Graham be bless with that magic power of fascination , by which a woman can charm with a word or intoxicate with a smile . every one love , admire , and praise she . the boy who open the five - bar gate that stand in her pathway , run home to his mother to tell of her pretty look , and the sweet voice in which she thank he for the little service . the verger at the church , who usher she into the surgeon 's pew ; the vicar , who see the soft blue eye uplifted to his face as he preach his simple sermon ; the porter from the railway station , who bring she sometimes a letter or a parcel , and who never look for reward from she ; her employer ; his visitor ; her pupil ; the servant ; everybody , high and low , unite in declare that Lucy Graham be the sweetest girl that ever live . 

 perhaps it be the rumor of this which penetrate into the quiet chamber of Audley Court ; or , perhaps , it be the sight of her pretty face , look over the surgeon 's high pew every Sunday morning ; however it be , it be certain that Sir Michael Audley suddenly experience a strong desire to be well acquaint with Mr. Dawson 's governess . 

 he have only to hint his wish to the worthy doctor for a little party to be get up , to which the vicar and his wife , and the baronet and his daughter , be invite . 

 that one quiet evening seal Sir Michael 's fate . he could no more resist the tender fascination of those soft and melt blue eye ; the graceful beauty of that slender throat and droop head , with its wealth of shower flaxen curl ; the low music of that gentle voice ; the perfect harmony which pervade every charm , and make all doubly charming in this woman ; than he could resist his destiny ! destiny ! why , she be his destiny ! he have never love before . what have be his marriage with Alicia 's mother but a dull , jog - trot bargain make to keep some estate in the family that would have be just as well out of it ? what have be his love for his first wife but a poor , pitiful , smolder spark , too dull to be extinguish , too feeble to burn ? but this be love this fever , this longing , this restless , uncertain , miserable hesitation ; these cruel fear that his age be an insurmountable barrier to his happiness ; this sick hatred of his white beard ; this frenzied wish to be young again , with glisten raven hair , and a slim waist , such as he have twenty year before ; these , wakeful night and melancholy day , so gloriously brighten if he chance to catch a glimpse of her sweet face behind the window curtain , as he drive past the surgeon 's house ; all these sign give token of the truth , and tell only too plainly that , at the sober age of fifty - five , Sir Michael Audley have fall ill of the terrible fever call love . 

 I do not think that , throughout his courtship , the baronet once calculate upon his wealth or his position as reason for his success . if he ever remember these thing , he dismiss the thought of they with a shudder . it pain he too much to believe for a moment that any one so lovely and innocent could value herself against a splendid house or a good old title . no ; his hope be that , as her life have be most likely one of toil and dependence , and as she be very young nobody exactly know her age , but she look little more than twenty , she might never have form any attachment , and that he , be the first to woo she , might , by tender attention , by generous watchfulness , by a love which should recall to she the father she have lose , and by a protect care that should make he necessary to she , win her young heart , and obtain from her fresh and early love , the promise of her hand . it be a very romantic day - dream , no doubt ; but , for all that , it seem in a very fair way to be realize . Lucy Graham appear by no mean to dislike the baronet 's attention . there be nothing whatever in her manner that betray the shallow artifice employ by a woman who wish to captivate a rich man . she be so accustomed to admiration from every one , high and low , that Sir Michael 's conduct make very little impression upon she . again , he have be so many year a widower that people have give up the idea of his ever marry again . at last , however , Mrs. Dawson speak to the governess on the subject . the surgeon 's wife be sit in the school - room busy at work , while Lucy be put the finishing touch on some water - color sketch do by her pupil . 

 " do you know , my dear Miss Graham , " say Mrs. Dawson , " I think you ought to consider yourself a remarkably lucky girl ? " 

 the governess lift her head from its stoop attitude , and star wonderingly at her employer , shake back a shower of curl . they be the most wonderful curl in the world soft and feathery , always float away from her face , and make a pale halo round her head when the sunlight shine through they . 

 " what do you mean , my dear Mrs. Dawson ? " she ask , dip her camel' - hair brush into the wet aquamarine upon the palette , and poise it carefully before put in the delicate streak of purple which be to brighten the horizon in her pupil 's sketch . 

 " why , I mean , my dear , that it only rest with yourself to become Lady Audley , and the mistress of Audley Court . " 

 Lucy Graham drop the brush upon the picture , and flush scarlet to the root of her fair hair ; and then grow pale again , far pale than Mrs. Dawson have ever see she before . 

 " my dear , do not   agitate yourself , " say the surgeon 's wife , soothingly ; " you know that nobody ask you to marry Sir Michael unless you wish . of course it would be a magnificent match ; he have a splendid income , and be one of the most generous of man . your position would be very high , and you would be enable to do a great deal of good ; but , as I say before , you must be entirely guide by your own feeling . only one thing I must say , and that be that if Sir Michael 's attention be not agreeable to you , it be really scarcely honorable to encourage he . " 

 " his attention encourage he ! " mutter Lucy , as if the word bewilder she . " pray , pray do not   talk to I , Mrs. Dawson . I have no idea of this . it be the last thing that would have occur to I . " she lean her elbow on the drawing - board before she , and clasp her hand over her face , seem for some minute to be think deeply . she wear a narrow black ribbon round her neck , with a locket , or a cross , or a miniature , perhaps , attach to it ; but whatever the trinket be , she always keep it hide under her dress . once or twice , while she sit silently think , she remove one of her hand from before her face , and fidget nervously with the ribbon , clutch at it with a half - angry gesture , and twist it backward and forward between her finger . 

 " I think some people be bear to be unlucky , Mrs. Dawson , " she say , by - and - by ; " it would be a great deal too much good fortune for I to become Lady Audley . " 

 she say this with so much bitterness in her tone , that the surgeon 's wife look up at she with surprise . 

 " you unlucky , my dear ! " she exclaim . " I think you be the last person who ought to talk like that you , such a bright , happy creature , that it do every one good to see you . I be sure I do not   know what we shall do if Sir Michael rob we of you . " 

 after this conversation they often speak upon the subject , and Lucy never again show any emotion whatever when the baronet 's admiration for she be canvass . it be a tacitly understand thing in the surgeon 's family that whenever Sir Michael propose , the governess would quietly accept he ; and , indeed , the simple Dawsons would have think it something more than madness in a penniless girl to reject such an offer . 

 so , one misty August evening , Sir Michael , sit opposite to Lucy Graham , at a window in the surgeon 's little drawing - room , take an opportunity while the family happen by some accident to be absent from the room , of speak upon the subject near to his heart . he make the governess , in a few but solemn word , an offer of his hand . there be something almost touch in the manner and tone in which he speak to her half in deprecation , know that he could hardly expect to be the choice of a beautiful young girl , and pray rather that she would reject he , even though she break his heart by do so , than that she should accept his offer if she do not love he . 

 " I scarcely think there be a great sin , Lucy , " he say , solemnly , " than that of a woman who marry a man she do not love . you be so precious to I , my beloved , that deeply as my heart be set on this , and bitter as the mere thought of disappointment be to I , I would not have you commit such a sin for any happiness of mine . if my happiness could be achieve by such an act , which it could not which it never could , " he repeat , earnestly " nothing but misery can result from a marriage dictate by any motive but truth and love . " 

 Lucy Graham be not look at Sir Michael , but straight out into the misty twilight and dim landscape far away beyond the little garden . the baronet try to see her face , but her profile be turn to he , and he could not discover the expression of her eye . if he could have do so , he would have see a yearn gaze which seem as if it would have pierce the far obscurity and look away away into another world . 

 " Lucy , you hear I ? " 

 " yes , " she say , gravely ; not coldly , or in any way as if she be offend at his word . 

 " and your answer ? " 

 she do not remove her gaze from the darken country side , but for some moment be quite silent ; then turn to he , with a sudden passion in her manner , that light up her face with a new and wonderful beauty which the baronet perceive even in the grow twilight , she fall on her knee at his foot . 

 " no , Lucy ; no , no ! " he cry , vehemently , " not here , not here ! " 

 " yes , here , here , " she say , the strange passion which agitate she make her voice sound shrill and pierce not loud , but preternaturally distinct ; " here and nowhere else . how good you be how noble and how generous ! love you ! why , there be woman a hundred time my superior in beauty and in goodness who might love you dearly ; but you ask too much of I ! remember what my life have be ; only remember that ! from my very babyhood I have never see anything but poverty . my father be a gentleman : clever , accomplished , handsome but poor and what a pitiful wretch poverty make of he ! my mother but do not let I speak of she . poverty poverty , trial , vexation , humiliation , deprivation . you can not tell ; you , who be among those for whom life be so smooth and easy , you can never guess what be endure by such as we . do not ask too much of I , then . I can not be disintereste ; I can not be blind to the advantage of such an alliance . I can not , I can not ! " 

 beyond her agitation and her passionate vehemence , there be an undefined something in her manner which fill the baronet with a vague alarm . she be still on the ground at his foot , crouch rather than kneeling , her thin white dress cling about she , her pale hair stream over her shoulder , her great blue eye glitter in the dusk , and her hand clutch at the black ribbon about her throat , as if it have be strangle she . " do not   ask too much of I , " she keep repeat ; " I have be selfish from my babyhood . " 

 " Lucy Lucy , speak plainly . do you dislike I ? " 

 " dislike you ? no no ! " 

 " but be there any one else whom you love ? " 

 she laugh aloud at his question . " I do not love any one in the world , " she answer . 

 he be glad of her reply ; and yet that and the strange laugh jar upon his feeling . he be silent for some moment , and then say , with a kind of effort : 

 " well , Lucy , I will not ask too much of you . I dare say I be a romantic old fool ; but if you do not dislike I , and if you do not love any one else , I see no reason why we should not make a very happy couple . be it a bargain , Lucy ? " 

 " yes . " 

 the baronet lift she in his arm and kiss she once upon the forehead , then quietly bid her good - night , he walk straight out of the house . 

 he walk straight out of the house , this foolish old man , because there be some strong emotion at work in his breast neither joy nor triumph , but something almost akin to disappointment some stifle and unsatisfie longing which lie heavy and dull at his heart , as if he have carry a corpse in his bosom . he carry the corpse of that hope which have die at the sound of Lucy 's word . all the doubt and fear and timid aspiration be end now . he must be content , like other man of his age , to be marry for his fortune and his position . 

 Lucy Graham go slowly up the stair to her little room at the top of the house . she place her dim candle on the chest of drawer , and seat herself on the edge of the white bed , still and white as the drapery hang around she . 

 " no more dependence , no more drudgery , no more humiliation , " she say ; " every trace of the old life melt away every clew to identity bury and forget except these , except these . " 

 she have never take her left hand from the black ribbon at her throat . she draw it from her bosom , as she speak , and look at the object attach to it . 

 it be neither a locket , a miniature , nor a cross ; it be a ring wrap in an oblong piece of paper the paper partly write , partly print , yellow with age , and crumple with much folding . 

 CHAPTER II . 

 ON BOARD THE ARGUS . 

 he throw the end of his cigar into the water , and lean his elbow upon the bulwark , star meditatively at the wave . 

 " how wearisome they be , " he say ; " blue and green , and opal ; opal , and blue , and green ; all very well in their way , of course , but three month of they be rather too much , especially " 

 he do not attempt to finish his sentence ; his thought seem to wander in the very midst of it , and carry he a thousand mile or so away . 

 " poor little girl , how pleased she will be ! " he mutter , open his cigar - case , lazily survey its content ; " how pleased and how surprised ? poor little girl . after three year and a half , too ; she will be surprised . " 

 he be a young man of about five - and - twenty , with dark face bronze by exposure to the sun ; he have handsome brown eye , with a lazy smile in they that sparkle through the black lash , and a bushy beard and mustache that cover the whole low part of his face . he be tall and powerfully build ; he wear a loose gray suit and a feel hat , throw carelessly upon his black hair . his name be George Talboys , and he be aft - cabin passenger on board the good ship Argus , laden with australian wool and sailing from Sydney to Liverpool . 

 there be very few passenger in the aft - cabin of the Argus . an elderly wool - stapler return to his native country with his wife and daughter , after have make a fortune in the colony ; a governess of three - and - thirty year of age , go home to marry a man to whom she have be engage fifteen year ; the sentimental daughter of a wealthy australian wine - merchant , invoice to England to finish her education , and George Talboys , be the only first - class passenger on board . 

 this George Talboys be the life and soul of the vessel ; nobody know who or what he be , or where he come from , but everybody like he . he sit at the bottom of the dinner - table , and assist the captain in do the honor of the friendly meal . he open the champagne bottle , and take wine with every one present ; he tell funny story , and lead the life himself with such a joyous peal that the man must have be a churl who could not have laugh for pure sympathy . he be a capital hand at speculation and vingt - et - un , and all the merry game , which keep the little circle round the cabin - lamp so deep in innocent amusement , that a hurricane might have howl overhead without their hearing it ; but he freely own that he have no talent for whist , and that he do not   know a knight from a castle upon the chess - board . 

 indeed , Mr. Talboys be by no mean too learn a gentleman . the pale governess have try to talk to he about fashionable literature , but George have only pull his beard and star very hard at she , say occasionally , " ah , yes , by Jove ! " and " to be sure , ah ! " 

 the sentimental young lady , go home to finish her education , have try he with Shelby and Byron , and he have fairly laugh in her face , as if poetry be a joke . the woolstapler sound he on politic , but he do not seem very deeply verse in they ; so they let he go his own way , smoke his cigar and talk to the sailor , lounge over the bulwark and stare at the water , and make himself agreeable to everybody in his own fashion . but when the Argus come to be within about a fortnight 's sail of England everybody notice a change in George Talboys . he grow restless and fidgety ; sometimes so merry that the cabin ring with his laughter ; sometimes moody and thoughtful . favorite as he be among the sailor , they be tired at last of answer his perpetual question about the probable time of touch land . would it be in ten day , in eleven , in twelve , in thirteen ? be the wind favorable ? how many knot an hour be the vessel do ? then a sudden passion would sieze he , and he would stamp upon the deck , cry out that she be a rickety old craft , and that her owner be swindler to advertise she as the fast - sailing Argus . she be not fit for passenger traffic ; she be not fit to carry impatient living creature , with heart and soul ; she be fit for nothing but to be laden with bale of stupid wool , that might rot on the sea and be none the bad for it . 

 the sun be droop down behind the wave as George Talboys light his cigar upon this August evening . only ten day more , the sailor have tell he that afternoon , and they would see the english coast . " I will go ashore in the first boat that hail we , " he cry ; " I will go ashore in a cockle - shell . by Jove , if it come to that , I will swim to land . " 

 his friend in the aft - cabin , with the exception of the pale governess , laugh at his impatience ; she sigh as she watch the young man , chafe at the slow hour , push away his untasted wine , fling himself restlessly about upon the cabin sofa , rush up and down the companion ladder , and star at the wave . 

 as the red rim of the sun drop into the water , the governess ascend the cabin stair for a stroll on deck , while the passenger sit over their wine below . she stop when she come up to George , and , stand by his side , watch the fade crimson in the western sky . 

 the lady be very quiet and reserved , seldom share in the after - cabin amusement , never laugh , and speak very little ; but she and George Talboys have be excellent friend throughout the passage . 

 " do my cigar annoy you , Miss Morley ? " he say , take it out of his mouth . 

 " not at all ; pray do not leave off smoking . I only come up to look at the sunset . what a lovely evening ! " 

 " yes , yes , I dare say , " he answer , impatiently ; " yet so long , so long ! ten more interminable day and ten more weary night before we land . " 

 " yes , " say Miss Morley , sigh . " do you wish the time short ? " 

 " do I ? " cry George . " indeed I do . do not   you ? " 

 " Scarcely . " 

 " but be there no one you love in England ? be there no one you love look out for your arrival ? " 

 " I hope so , " she say gravely . they be silent for some time , he smoke his cigar with a furious impatience , as if he could hasten the course of the vessel by his own restlessness ; she look out at the wan light with melancholy blue eye eye that seem to have fade with pore over closely - print book and difficult needlework ; eye that have fade a little , perhaps , by reason of tear secretly shed in the lonely night . 

 " see ! " say George , suddenly , point in another direction from that toward which Miss Morley be look , " there be the new moon ! " 

 she look up at the pale crescent , her own face almost as pale and wan . 

 " this be the first time we have see it . " 

 " we must wish ! " say George . " I know what I wish . " 

 " what ? " 

 " that we may get home quickly . " 

 " my wish be that we may find no disappointment when we get there , " say the governess , sadly . 

 " Disappointment ! " 

 he start as if he have be strike , and ask what she mean by talk of disappointment . 

 " I mean this , " she say , speak rapidly , and with a restless motion of her thin hand ; " I mean that as the end of the voyage draw near , hope sink in my heart ; and a sick fear come over I that at the last all may not be well . the person I go to meet may be change in his feeling toward I ; or he may retain all the old feeling until the moment of see I , and then lose it in a breath at sight of my poor wan face , for I be call a pretty girl , Mr. Talboys , when I sail for Sydney , fifteen year ago ; or he may be so change by the world as to have grow selfish and mercenary , and he may welcome I for the sake of my fifteen year ' saving . again , he may be dead . he may have be well , perhaps , up to within a week of our landing , and in that last week may have take a fever , and die an hour before our vessel anchor in the Mersey . I think of all these thing , Mr. Talboys , and act the scene over in my mind , and feel the anguish of they twenty time a day . twenty time a day , " she repeat ; " why I do it a thousand time a day . " 

 George Talboys have stand motionless , with his cigar in his hand , listen to she so intently that , as she say the last word , his hold relax , and the cigar drop in the water . 

 " I wonder , " she continue , more to herself than to he , " I wonder , look back , to think how hopeful I be when the vessel sail ; I never think then of disappointment , but I picture the joy of meeting , imagine the very word that would be say , the very tone , the very look ; but for this last month of the voyage , day by day , and hour by hour my heart sink and my hopeful fancy fade away , and I dread the end as much as if I know that I be go to England to attend a funeral . " 

 the young man suddenly change his attitude , and turn his face full upon his companion , with a look of alarm . she see in the pale light that the color have fade from his cheek . 

 " what a fool ! " he cry , strike his clench fist upon the side of the vessel , " what a fool I be to be frighten at this ? why do you come and say these thing to I ? why do you come and terrify I out of my sens , when I be go straight home to the woman I love ; to a girl whose heart be as true as the light of Heaven ; and in whom I no more expect to find any change than I do to see another sun rise in to - morrow 's sky ? why do you come and try to put such fancy in my head when I be go home to my darling wife ? " 

 " your wife , " she say ; " that be different . there be no reason that my terror should terrify you . I be go to England to rejoin a man to whom I be engage to be marry fifteen year ago . he be too poor to marry then , and when I be offer a situation as governess in a rich australian family , I persuade he to let I accept it , so that I might leave he free and unfettered to win his way in the world , while I save a little money to help we when we begin life together . I never mean to stay away so long , but thing have go badly with he in England . that be my story , and you can understand my fear . they need not influence you . mine be an exceptional case . " 

 " so be mine , " say George , impatiently . " I tell you that mine be an exceptional case : although I swear to you that until this moment , I have never know a fear as to the result of my voyage home . but you be right ; your terror have nothing to do with I . you have be away fifteen year ; all kind of thing may happen in fifteen year . now it be only three year and a half this very month since I leave England . what can have happen in such a short time as that ? " 

 Miss Morley look at he with a mournful smile , but do not speak . his feverish ardor , the freshness and impatience of his nature be so strange and new to she , that she look at he half in admiration , half in pity . 

 " my pretty little wife ! my gentle , innocent , love little wife ! do you know , Miss Morley , " he say , with all his old hopefulness of manner , " that I leave my little girl asleep , with her baby in her arm , and with nothing but a few blot line to tell she why her faithful husband have desert she ? " 

 " desert she ! " exclaim the governess . 

 " yes . I be an ensign in a cavalry regiment when I first meet my little darling . we be quarter at a stupid seaport town , where my pet live with her shabby old father , a half - pay naval officer ; a regular old humbug , as poor as Job , and with an eye for nothing but the main chance . I see through all his shallow trick to catch one of we for his pretty daughter . I see all the pitiable , contemptible , palpable trap he set for we big dragoon to walk into . I see through his shabby - genteel dinner and public - house port ; his fine talk of the grandeur of his family ; his sham pride and independence , and the sham tear of his bleared old eye when he talk of his only child . he be a drunken old hypocrite , and he be ready to sell my poor , little girl to the high bidder . luckily for I , I happen just then to be the high bidder ; for my father , be a rich man , Miss Morley , and as it be love at first sight on both side , my darling and I make a match of it . no soon , however , do my father hear that I have marry a penniless little girl , the daughter of a tipsy old half - pay lieutenant , than he write I a furious letter , tell I he would never again hold any communication with I , and that my yearly allowance would stop from my wedding - day . 

 " as there be no remain in such a regiment as mine , with nothing but my pay to live on , and my pretty little wife to keep , I sell out , think that before the money be exhaust , I should be sure to drop into something . I take my darling to Italy , and we live there in splendid style as long as my two thousand pound last ; but when that begin to dwindle down to a couple of hundred or so , we come back to England , and as my darling have a fancy for be near that tiresome old father of her , we settle at the watering - place where he live . well , as soon as the old man hear that I have a couple of hundred pound leave , he express a wonderful degree of affection for we , and insist on our boarding in his house . we consent , still to please my darling , who have just then a peculiar right to have every whim and fancy of her innocent heart indulge . we do board with he , and finally he fleece we ; but when I speak of it to my little wife , she only shrug her shoulder , and say she do not like to be unkind to her ' poor papa . ' so poor papa make away with our little stock of money in no time ; and as I feel that it be now become necessary to look about for something , I run up to London , and try to get a situation as a clerk in a merchant 's office , or as accountant , or book - keeper , or something of that kind . but I suppose there be the stamp of a heavy dragoon about I , for do what I would I could not   get anybody to believe in my capacity ; and tired out , and down - hearte , I return to my darling , to find she nursing a son and heir to his father 's poverty . poor little girl , she be very low - spirited ; and when I tell she that my London expedition have fail , she fairly break down , and burst in to a storm of sob and lamentation , tell I that I ought not to have marry she if I could give she nothing but poverty and misery ; and that I have do she a cruel wrong in make she my wife . by heaven ! Miss Morley , her tear and reproach drive I almost mad ; and I fly into a rage with she , myself , her father , the world , and everybody in it , and then rail out of the house . I walk about the street all that day , half out of my mind , and with a strong inclination to throw myself into the sea , so as to leave my poor girl free to make a well match . ' if I drown myself , her father must support she , ' I think ; ' the old hypocrite could never refuse she a shelter ; but while I live she have no claim on he . ' I go down to a rickety old wooden pier , mean to wait there till it be dark , and then drop quietly over the end of it into the water ; but while I sit there smoke my pipe , and star vacantly at the sea - gull , two man come down , and one of they begin to talk of the australian gold - digging , and the great thing that be to be do there . it appear that he be go to sail in a day or two , and he be try to persuade his companion to join he in the expedition . 

 " I listen to these man for upward of an hour , follow they up and down the pier , with my pipe in my mouth , and hear all their talk . after this I fall into conversation with they myself , and ascertain that there be a vessel go to leave Liverpool in three day , by which vessel one of the man be go out . this man give I all the information I require , and tell I , moreover , that a stalwart young fellow , such as I be , could hardly fail to do well in the digging . the thought flash upon I so suddenly , that I grow hot and red in the face , and tremble in every limb with excitement . this be well than the water , at any rate . suppose I steal away from my darling , leave she safe under her father 's roof , and go and make a fortune in the new world , and come back in a twelvemonth to throw it into her lap ; for I be so sanguine in those day that I count on make my fortune in a year or so . I thank the man for his information , and late at night stroll homeward . it be bitter winter weather , but I have be too full of passion to feel cold , and I walk through the quiet street , with the snow drift in my face , and a desperate hopefulness in my heart . the old man be sit drink brandy - and - water in the little dining - room ; and my wife be up - stair , sleep peacefully , with the baby on her breast . I sit down and write a few brief line , which tell she that I never have love she well than now , when I seem to desert she ; that I be go to try my fortune in the new world , and that if I succeed I should come back to bring she plenty and happiness ; but that if I fail I should never look upon her face again . I divide the remainder of our money something over forty pound into two equal portion , leave one for she , and put the other in my pocket . I kneel down and pray for my wife and child , with my head upon the white counterpane that cover they . I be not   much of a pray man at ordinary time , but God know that be a heartfelt prayer . I kiss she once , and the baby once , and then creep out of the room . the dining - room door be open , and the old man be nod over his paper . he look up as he hear my step in the passage , and ask I where I be go . ' to have a smoke in the street , ' I answer ; and as this be a common habit of mine he believe I . three night after I be out at sea , bind for Melbourne a steerage passenger , with a digger 's tool for my baggage , and about seven shilling in my pocket . " 

 " and you succeed ? " ask Miss Morley . 

 " not till I have long despair of success ; not until poverty and I have become such old companion and bed - fellow , that look back at my past life , I wonder whether that dashing , reckless , extravagant , luxurious , champagne - drink dragoon could have really be the same man who sit on the damp ground gnaw a moldy crust in the wild of the new world . I cling to the memory of my darling , and the trust that I have in her love and truth be the one keystone that keep the fabric of my past life together the one star that light the thick black darkness of the future . I be hail - fellow - well - meet with bad man ; I be in the center of riot , drunkenness , and debauchery ; but the purify influence of my love keep I safe from all . thin and gaunt , the half - starve shadow of what I once have be , I see myself one day in a broken bit of look - glass , and be frighten by my own face . but I toil on through all ; through disappointment and despair , rheumatism , fever , starvation ; at the very gate of death , I toil on steadily to the end ; and in the end I conquer . " 

 he be so brave in his energy and determination , in his proud triumph of success , and in the knowledge of the difficulty he have vanquish , that the pale governess could only look at he in wonder admiration . 

 " how brave you be ! " she say . 

 " Brave ! " he cry , with a joyous peal of laughter ; " be not   I work for my darling ? through all the dreary time of that probation , her pretty white hand seem beckon I onward to a happy future ! why , I have see she under my wretched canvas tent sit by my side , with her boy in her arm , as plainly as I have ever see she in the one happy year of our wed life . at last , one dreary foggy morning , just three month ago , with a drizzle rain wet I to the skin , up to my neck in clay and mire , half - starve , enfeeble by fever , stiff with rheumatism , a monster nugget turn up under my spade , and I be in one minute the rich man in Australia . I fall down on the wet clay , with my lump of gold in the bosom of my shirt , and , for the first time in my life , cry like a child . I travel post - haste to Sydney , realize my price , which be worth upward of L20,000 , and a fortnight afterward take my passage for England in this vessel ; and in ten day in ten day I shall see my darling . " 

 " but in all that time do you never write to your wife ? " 

 " never , till the night before I leave Sydney . I could not write when everything look so black . I could not write and tell she that I be fight hard with despair and death . I wait for well fortune , and when that come I write tell she that I should be in England almost as soon as my letter , and give she an address at a coffee - house in London where she could write to I , tell I where to find she , though she be hardly likely to have leave her father 's house . " 

 he fall into a reverie after this , and puff meditatively at his cigar . his companion do not disturb he . the last ray of summer daylight have die out , and the pale light of the crescent moon only remain . 

 presently George Talboys fling away his cigar , and turn to the governess , cry abruptly , " Miss Morley , if , when I get to England , I hear that anything have happen to my wife , I shall fall down dead . " 

 " my dear Mr. Talboys , why do you think of these thing ? God be very good to we ; he will not afflict we beyond our power of endurance . I see all thing , perhaps , in a melancholy light ; for the long monotony of my life have give I too much time to think over my trouble . " 

 " and my life have be all action , privation , toil , alternate hope and despair ; I have have no time to think upon the chance of anything happen to my darling . what a blind , reckless fool I have be ! three year and a half and not one line one word from she , or from any mortal creature who know she . Heaven above ! what may not have happen ? " 

 in the agitation of his mind he begin to walk rapidly up and down the lonely deck , the governess follow , and try to soothe he . 

 " I swear to you , Miss Morley , " he say , " that till you speak to I to - night , I never feel one shadow of fear , and now I have that sick , sink dread at my heart which you talk of an hour ago . let I alone , please , to get over it my own way . " 

 she draw silently away from he , and seat herself by the side of the vessel , look over into the water . 

 George Talboys walk backward and forward for some time , with his head bend upon his breast , look neither to the right nor the left , but in about a quarter of an hour he return to the spot where the governess be seat . 

 " I have be pray , " he say " pray for my darling . " 

 he speak in a voice little above a whisper , and she see his face ineffably calm in the moonlight . 

 CHAPTER III . 

 HIDDEN RELICS . 

 the same August sun which have go down behind the waste of water glimmer redly upon the broad face of the old clock over that ivy - cover archway which lead into the garden of Audley Court . 

 a fierce and crimson sunset . the mullione window and twinkle lattice be all ablaze with the red glory ; the fade light flicker upon the leaf of the lime in the long avenue , and change the still fish - pond into a sheet of burnish copper ; even into those dim recess of bri and brushwood , amidst which the old well be hide , the crimson brightness penetrate in fitful flash till the dank weed and the rusty iron wheel and broken woodwork seem as if they be fleck with blood . 

 the lowing of a cow in the quiet meadow , the splash of a trout in the fish - pond , the last note of a tired bird , the creaking of wagon - wheel upon the distant road , every now and then break the evening silence , only make the stillness of the place seem more intense . it be almost oppressive , this twilight stillness . the very repose of the place grow painful from its intensity , and you feel as if a corpse must be lie somewhere within that gray and ivy - cover pile of building so deathlike be the tranquillity of all around . 

 as the clock over the archway strike eight , a door at the back of the house be softly open , and a girl come out into the garden . 

 but even the presence of a human being scarcely break the silence ; for the girl creep slowly over the thick grass , and glide into the avenue by the side of the fish - pond , disappear in the rich shelter of the lime . 

 she be not , perhaps , positively a pretty girl ; but her appearance be of that order which be commonly call interesting . interesting , it may be , because in the pale face and the light gray eye , the small feature and compress lip , there be something which hint at a power of repression and self - control not common in a woman of nineteen or twenty . she might have be pretty , I think , but for the one fault in her small oval face . this fault be an absence of color . not one tinge of crimson flush the waxen whiteness of her cheek ; not one shadow of brown redeem the pale insipidity of her eyebrow and eyelash ; not one glimmer of gold or auburn relieve the dull flaxen of her hair . even her dress be spoil by this same deficiency . the pale lavender muslin fade into a sickly gray , and the ribbon knot round her throat melt into the same neutral hue . 

 her figure be slim and fragile , and in spite of her humble dress , she have something of the grace and carriage of a gentlewoman , but she be only a simple country girl , call Phoebe Marks , who have be nursemaid in Mr. Dawson 's family , and whom Lady Audley have choose for she maid after her marriage with Sir Michael . 

 of course , this be a wonderful piece of good fortune for Phoebe , who find her wage treble and her work lighten in the well - order household at the Court ; and who be therefore quite as much the object of envy among her particular friend as my lady herself to high circle . 

 a man , who be sit on the break wood - work of the well , start as the lady's - maid come out of the dim shade of the lime and stand before he among the weed and brushwood . 

 I have say before that this be a neglect spot ; it lie in the midst of a low shrubbery , hide away from the rest of the garden , and only visible from the garret window at the back of the west wing .

I have   come across through the field , and come in here at the gate agen the moat , and I be take a rest before I come up to the house to ask if you be come back . "

 the man be a big , broad - shoulder , stupid - look clod - hopper of about twenty - three year of age . his dark red hair grow low upon his forehead , and his bushy brow meet over a pair of greenish gray eye ; his nose be large and well - shape , but the mouth be coarse in form and animal in expression . rosy - cheeked , red - haired , and bull - neck , he be not unlike one of the stout oxen grazing in the meadow round about the Court .

 they be first cousin , and have be play fellow in childhood , and sweetheart in early youth .

 " Genteel ! " cry Luke Marks , with a hoarse laugh ; " who want you to be genteel , I wonder ? not I , for one ; when you be my wife you will not   have overmuch time for gentility , my girl . french , too ! dang I , Phoebe , I suppose when we have   save money enough between we to buy a bit of a farm , you will be parleyvooe to the cow ? "

" what be she but a servant like I ? take wage and work for they as hard , or hard , than I do . you should have see her shabby clothe , Luke wear and patch , and darn and turn and twist , yet always look nice upon she , somehow . she give I more as lady's - maid here than ever she get from Mr. Dawson then . why , I have   see she come out of the parlor with a few sovereign and a little silver in her hand , that master have just give she for her quarter 's salary ; and now look at she !  she lead the way through a great drawing - room , rich in satin and ormolu , buhl and inlay cabinet , bronze , cameo , statuette , and trinket , that glisten in the dusky light ; then through a morning room , hang with proof engraving of valuable picture ; through this into an ante - chamber , where she stop , hold the light above her head .

 CHAPTER IV . 

 in the first page of " the TIMES . " 

 Robert Audley be suppose to be a barrister . as a barrister be his name inscribe in the law - list ; as a barrister he have chamber in Figtree Court , Temple ; as a barrister he have eat the allotted number of dinner , which form the sublime ordeal through which the forensic aspirant wade on to fame and fortune . if these thing can make a man a barrister , Robert Audley decidedly be one . but he have never either have a brief , or try to get a brief , or even wish to have a brief in all those five year , during which his name have be paint upon one of the door in Figtree Court . he be a handsome , lazy , care - for - nothing fellow , of about seven - and - twenty ; the only son of a young brother of Sir Michael Audley . his father have leave he l400 a year , which his friend have advise he to increase by be call to the bar ; and as he find it , after due consideration , more trouble to oppose the wish of these friend than to eat so many dinner , and to take a set of chamber in the Temple , he adopt the latter course , and unblushingly call himself a barrister . 

 sometimes , when the weather be very hot , and he have exhaust himself with the exertion of smoke his german pipe , and read french novel , he would stroll into the Temple Gardens , and lie in some shady spot , pale and cool , with his shirt collar turn down and a blue silk handkerchief tie loosely about his neck , would tell grave bencher that he have knock himself up with over work . 

 the sly old bencher laugh at the pleasant fiction ; but they all agree that Robert Audley be a good fellow ; a generous - hearted fellow ; rather a curious fellow , too , with a fund of sly wit and quiet humor , under his listless , dawdling , indifferent , irresolute manner . a man who would never get on in the world ; but who would not hurt a worm . indeed , his chamber be convert into a perfect dog - kennel , by his habit of bring home stray and benighted cur , who be attract by his look in the street , and follow he with abject fondness . 

 Robert always spend the hunting season at Audley Court ; not that he be distinguish as a Nimrod , for he would quietly trot to covert upon a mild - temper , stout - limbe bay hack , and keep at a very respectful distance from the hard rider ; his horse know quite as well as he do , that nothing be further from his thought than any desire to be in at the death . 

 the young man be a great favorite with his uncle , and by no mean despise by his pretty , gipsy - face , light - hearted , hoydenish cousin , Miss Alicia Audley . it might have seem to other man , that the partiality of a young lady who be sole heiress to a very fine estate , be rather well worth cultivating , but it do not so occur to Robert Audley . Alicia be a very nice girl , he say , a jolly girl , with no nonsense about she a girl of a thousand ; but this be the high point to which enthusiasm could carry he . the idea of turn his cousin 's girlish like for he to some good account never enter his idle brain . I doubt if he even have any correct notion of the amount of his uncle 's fortune , and I be certain that he never for one moment calculate upon the chance of any part of that fortune ultimately come to himself . so that when , one fine spring morning , about three month before the time of which I be write , the postman bring he the wedding card of Sir Michael and Lady Audley , together with a very indignant letter from his cousin , set forth how her father have just marry a wax - dollish young person , no old than Alicia herself , with flaxen ringlet , and a perpetual giggle ; for I be sorry to say that Miss Audley 's animus cause she thus to describe that pretty musical laugh which have be so much admire in the late Miss Lucy Graham when , I say , these document reach Robert Audley they elicit neither vexation nor astonishment in the lymphatic nature of that gentleman . he read Alicia 's angry cross and recrossed letter without so much as remove the amber mouth - piece of his german pipe from his mustache lip . when he have finish the perusal of the epistle , which he read with his dark eyebrow elevate to the center of his forehead   his only manner of express surprise , by the way   he deliberately throw that and the wedding card into the waste - paper basket , and put down his pipe , prepare himself for the exertion of think out the subject . 

 " I always say the old buffer would marry , " he mutter , after about half an hour 's revery . Alicia and my lady , the stepmother , will go at it hammer and tong . I hope they will not   quarrel in the hunting season , or say unpleasant thing to each other at the dinner - table ; row always upset a man 's digestion . 

 at about twelve o'clock on the morning follow that night upon which the event record in my last chapter have take place , the baronet 's nephew stroll out of the Temple , Blackfriarsward , on his way to the city . he have in an evil hour oblige some necessitous friend by put the ancient name of Audley across a bill of accommodation , which bill not have be provide for by the drawer , Robert be call upon to pay . for this purpose he saunter up Ludgate Hill , with his blue necktie flutter in the hot August air , and thence to a refreshingly cool banking - house in a shady court out of St. Paul 's churchyard , where he make arrangement for sell out a couple of hundred pound ' worth of consol .

 " on the 24th inst . , at Ventnor , Isle of Wight , Helen Talboys , age 22 . " 

 CHAPTER V. 

 the headstone AT VENTNOR . 

 yes , there it be in black and white " Helen Talboys , age 22 . " 

 when George tell the governess on board the Argus that if he hear any evil tiding of his wife he should drop down dead , he speak in perfect good faith ; and yet , here be the bad tiding that could come to he , and he sit rigid , white and helpless , star stupidly at the shocked face of his friend .

 the hot August sunshine , the dusty window - pane and shabby - paint blind , a file of fly - blow play - bill fasten to the wall , the black and empty fire - place , a bald - headed old man nod over the Morning Advertizer , the slip - shod waiter fold a tumbled table - cloth , and Robert Audley 's handsome face look at he full of compassionate alarm he know that all these thing take gigantic proportion , and then , one by one , melt into dark blot and swam before his eye , he know that there be a great noise , as of half a dozen furious steam - engine tear and grind in his ear , and he know nothing more except that somebody or something fall heavily to the ground .

 " no . "

 " in my chamber , dear boy , in the Temple . you have no lodging of your own , so you may as well stay with I while you be in town . "

 " it may be a misprint for Talbot . "

 " to Ventnor , to see her grave . "

 " have we not well ask at one of the hotel about a Mrs. Talboys , George ? " he say .

 yes , they tell he , there be a gentleman of that name stop at Ventnor , a Captain Maldon ; his daughter be lately dead . the waiter would go and inquire for the address . 

 the hotel be a busy place at this season ; people hurry in and out , and a great bustle of groom and waiter about the hall .

 the bad be confirm now . his wife , Captain Maldon 's daughter be dead .

 it be his own portrait , paint in the old dragooning day . a pretty good likeness , represent he in uniform , with his charger in the background .

 her workbox , with an unfinished piece of work ; her album full of extract from Byron and Moore , write in his own scrawling hand ; some book which he have give she , and a bunch of wither flower in a vase they have buy in Italy .

 sacred to the Memory of 
 HELEN , 
 the beloved wife of GEORGE TALBOYS ,

 August 24th , 18 , age 22 , 
 deeply regret by her sorrow Husband . 

 CHAPTER VI . 

 ANYWHERE , ANYWHERE OUT of the world . 

 when they return to Lansdowne Cottage they find the old man have not yet come in , so they walk down to the beach to look for he . after a brief search they find he , sit upon a heap of pebble , read a newspaper and eat filbert . the little boy be at some distance from his grandfather , dig in the sand with a wooden spade . the crape round the old man 's shabby hat , and the child 's poor little black frock , go to George 's heart . go where he would he meet fresh confirmation of this great grief of his life . his wife be dead . 

 " Mr. Maldon , " he say , as he approach his father - in - law . 

 the old man look up , and , drop his newspaper , rise from the pebble with a ceremonious bow . his faded light hair be ting with gray ; he have a pinch hook nose ; watery blue eye , and an irresolute - look mouth ; he wear his shabby dress with an affectation of foppish gentility ; an eye - glass dangle over his closely button - up waistcoat , and he carry a cane in his ungloved hand . 

 " Great Heaven ! " cry George , " do not   you know I ? " 

 Mr. Maldon start and color violently , with something of a frightened look , as he recognize his son - in - law . 

 " my dear boy , " he say , " I do not ; for the first moment I do not . that beard make such a difference . you find the beard make a great difference , do you not , sir ? " he say , appeal to Robert . 

 " great heaven ! " exclaim George Talboys , " be this the way you welcome I ? I come to England to find my wife dead within a week of my touching land , and you begin to chatter to I about my beard you , her father ! "

 he begin to walk up and down the beach , his father - in - law look helplessly at he , rub his feeble eye with a handkerchief . 

 " I have   a strong notion that that old man do not   treat his daughter too well , " think Robert , as he watch the half - pay lieutenant . " he seem , for some reason or other , to be half afraid of George . " 

 while the agitated young man walk up and down in a fever of regret and despair , the child run to his grandfather , and clung about the tail of his coat . 

 " come home , grandpa , come home , " he say . " I be tired . " 

 George Talboys turn at the sound of the babyish voice , and look long and earnestly at the boy . 

 he have his father 's brown eye and dark hair .

 the little fellow push he away . " I do not   know you , " he say . " I love grandpa and Mrs. Monks at Southampton . "

 " the boy seem fond of you , Mr. Maldon , " say George , after a pause .

 " then he have well stop with you . the interest of my money will be about six hundred a year . you can draw a hundred of that for Georgey 's education , leave the rest to accumulate till he be of age . my friend here will be trustee , and if he will undertake the charge , I will appoint he guardian to the boy , allow he for the present to remain under your care . " 

 " but why not take care of he yourself , George ? " ask Robert Audley . 

 " because I shall sail in the very next vessel that leave Liverpool for Australia . I shall be well in the digging or the backwood than ever I could be here . I be break for a civilized life from this hour , Bob . " 

 the old man 's weak eye sparkle as George declare this determination . 

 " my poor boy , I think you be right , " he say , " I really think you be right . the change , the wild life , the the " he hesitate and break down as Robert look earnestly at he . 

 " you be in a great hurry to get rid of your son - in - law , I think , Mr. Maldon , " he say , gravely . 

 " get rid of he , dear boy ! oh , no , no ! but for his own sake , my dear sir , for his own sake , you know . " 

 " I think for his own sake he would much well stay in England and look after his son , " say Robert . 

 " but I tell you I can not , " cry George ; " every inch of this accursed ground be hateful to I I want to run out of it as I would out of a graveyard . I will go back to town to - night , get that business about the money settle early to - morrow morning , and start for Liverpool without a moment 's delay . I shall be well when I have   put half the world between I and her grave . " 

 " before he leave the house he steal out to the landlady , and ask some more question about his dead wife . 

 " be they poor ? " he ask , " be they pinch for money while she be ill ? " 

 " oh , no ! " the woman answer ; " though the captain dress shabby , he have always plenty of sovereign in his purse . the poor lady want for nothing . " 

 George be relieve at this , though it puzzle he to know where the drunken half - pay lieutenant could have contrive to find money for all the expense of his daughter 's illness . 

 but he be too thoroughly break down by the calamity which have befall he to be able to think much of anything , so he ask no further question , but walk with his father - in - law and Robert Audley down to the boat by which they be to cross to Portsmouth . 

 the old man bid Robert a very ceremonious adieu .

George star at he , mutter something indistinct , and run down the ladder to the boat before Mr. Maldon could repeat his request . the steamer speed away through the sunset , and the outline of the island melt in the horizon as they near the opposite shore .

 the document which appoint Robert Audley as guardian to little George Talboys be draw up in a solicitor 's office the next morning .

Grief be so selfish that George do not know the sacrifice his friend make for his comfort . he only know that for he the sun be darken , and the business of life do . he sit all day long smoking cigar , and star at the flower and canary , chafe for the time to pass that he might be far out at sea .

 this be not a very cheerful way of look at thing , but Robert Audley be quite satisfied with have win his consent . 

 the three young man start under very favorable circumstance , carry letter of introduction to the most influential inhabitant of the russian capital . 

 before leave England , Robert write to his cousin Alicia , tell she of his intend departure with his old friend George Talboys , whom he have lately meet for the first time after a lapse of year , and who have just lose his wife . 

 Alicia 's reply come by return post , and run thus : 

 " MY dear robert how cruel of you to run away to that horrid St. Petersburg before the hunting season ! I have hear that people lose their nose in that disagreeable climate , and as yours be rather a long one , I should advise you to return before the very severe weather set in . what sort of person be this Mr. Talboys ? if he be very agreeable you may bring he to the Court as soon as you return from your travel . Lady Audley tell I to request you to secure she a set of sable . you be not to consider the price , but to be sure that they be the handsome that can be obtain . Papa be perfectly absurd about his new wife , and she and I can not get on together at all ; not that she be , disagreeable to I , for , as far as that go , she make herself agreeable to every one ; but she be so irretrievably childish and silly . 

 " believe I to be , my dear Robert . 

 " your affectionate cousin , 

 " ALICIA AUDLEY . " 

 CHAPTER VII . 

 after a year . 

 the first year of George Talboys ' widowhood pass away , the deep band of crepe about his hat grow brown and dusty , and as the last burn day of another August fade out , he sit smoke cigar in the quiet chamber of Figtree Court , much as he have do the year before , when the horror of his grief be new to he , and every object in life , however trifle or however important , seem saturate with his one great sorrow . 

 but the big ex - dragoon have survive his affliction by a twelvemonth , and hard as it may be to have to tell it , he do not look much the bad for it . Heaven know what waste agony of remorse and self - reproach may not have rack George 's honest heart , as he lie awake at night think of the wife he have abandon in the pursuit of a fortune , which she never live to share . 

 once , while they be abroad , Robert Audley venture to congratulate he upon his recovered spirit . he burst into a bitter laugh . 

 " do you know , Bob , " he say , " that when some of our fellow be wound in India , they come home , bring bullet inside they . they do not talk of they , and they be stout and hearty , and look as well , perhaps , as you or I ; but every change in the weather , however slight , every variation of the atmosphere , however trifle , bring back the old agony of their wound as sharp as ever they have feel it on the battle - field . I have   have my wound , Bob ; I carry the bullet still , and I shall carry it into my coffin . " 

 the traveler return from St. Petersburg in the spring , and George again take up his quarter at his old friend 's chamber , only leave they now and then to run down to Southampton and take a look at his little boy . he always go load with toy and sweetmeat to give to the child ; but , for all this , Georgey would not become very familiar with his papa , and the young man 's heart sicken as he begin to fancy that even his child be lose to he . 

 " what can I do ? " he think . " if I take he away from his grandfather , I shall break his heart ; if I let he remain , he will grow up a stranger to I , and care more for that drunken old hypocrite than for his own father . but then , what could an ignorant , heavy dragoon like I do with such a child ? what could I teach he , except to smoke cigar and idle around all day with his hand in his pocket ? " 

 so the anniversary of that 30th of August , upon which George have see the advertisement of his wife 's death in the Times newspaper , come round for the first time , and the young man put off his black clothe and the shabby crape from his hat , and lay his mournful garment in a trunk in which he keep a packet of his wife 's letter , her portrait , and that lock of hair which have be cut from her head after death . Robert Audley have never see either the letter , the portrait , or the long tress of silky hair ; nor , indeed , have George ever mention the name of his dead wife after that one day at Ventnor , on which he learn the full particular of her decease . 

 " I shall write to my cousin Alicia to - day , George , " the young barrister say , upon this very 30th of August . " do you know that the day after to - morrow be the 1st of September ? I shall write and tell she that we will both run down to the Court for a week 's shooting . " 

 " no , no , Bob ; go by yourself ; they do not   want I , and I would rather " 

 " bury yourself in Figtree Court , with no company but my dog and canary ! no , George , you shall do nothing of the kind . " 

 " but I do not   care for shoot . " 

 " and do you suppose I care for it ? " cry Robert , with charming naivete . " why , man , I do not   know a partridge from a pigeon , and it might be the 1st of April , instead of the 1st of September , for aught I care . I never hurt a bird in my life , but I have hurt my own shoulder with the weight of my gun . I only go down to Essex for the change of air , the good dinner , and the sight of my uncle 's honest , handsome face . besides , this time I have   another inducement , as I want to see this fair - haired paragon my new aunt . you will go with I , George ? " 

 " yes , if you really wish it . " 

 the quiet form his grief have take after its first brief violence , leave he as submissive as a child to the will of his friend ; ready to go anywhere or do anything ; never enjoy himself , or originate any enjoyment , but join in the pleasure of other with a hopeless , uncomplaine , unobtrusive resignation peculiar to his simple nature . but the return of post bring a letter from Alicia Audley , to say that the two young man could not be receive at the Court . 

 " there be seventeen spare bed - room , " write the young lady , in an indignant run hand , " but for all that , my dear Robert , you can not   come ; for my lady have take it into her silly head that she be too ill to entertain visitor   there be no more the matter with she than there be with I , and she can not have gentleman   great , rough man , she say   in the house . please apologize to your friend Mr. Talboys , and tell he that papa expect to see you both in the hunting season . " 

 " my lady 's air and grace shall not   keep we out of Essex for all that , " say Robert , as he twist the letter into a pipe - light for his big meerschaum . " I will tell you what we will do , George : there be a glorious inn at Audley , and plenty of fishing in the neighborhood ; we will go there and have a week 's sport . fishing be much well than shoot ; you have   only to lie on a bank and stare at your line ; I do not   find that you often catch anything , but it be very pleasant . " 

 he hold the twisted letter to the feeble spark of fire glimmering in the grate , as he speak , and then change his mind , deliberately unfold it , and smooth the crumpled paper with his hand .

" he say , thoughtfully ; " it be rather hard to treat her letter so cavalierly I will keep it ; " upon which Mr. Robert Audley put the note back into its envelope , and afterward thrust it into a pigeon - hole in his office desk , mark important . Heaven know what wonderful document there be in this particular pigeon - hole , but I do not think it likely to have contain anything of great judicial value . if any one could at that moment have tell the young barrister that so simple a thing as his cousin 's brief letter would one day come to be a link in that terrible chain of evidence afterward to be slowly forge in the only criminal case in which he be ever to be concern , perhaps Mr. Robert Audley would have lift his eyebrow a little high than usual .

 Audley Court be about three - quarter of a mile from the village , lying , as I have say , deep down in the hollow , shut in by luxuriant timber . you could only reach it by a cross - road border by tree , and as trimly keep as the avenue in a gentleman 's park . it be a lonely place enough , even in all its rustic beauty , for so bright a creature as the late Miss Lucy Graham , but the generous baronet have transform the interior of the gray old mansion into a little palace for his young wife , and Lady Audley seem as happy as a child surround by new and costly toy .

George Talboys feel this , and in this he experience the near approach to enjoyment that he have ever know since his wife 's death .

 we hear every day of murder commit in the country . brutal and treacherous murder ; slow , protract agony from poison administer by some kindre hand ; sudden and violent death by cruel blow , inflict with a stake cut from some spread oak , whose every shadow promise peace . in the county of which I write , I have be show a meadow in which , on a quiet summer Sunday evening , a young farmer murder the girl who have love and trust he ; and yet , even now , with the stain of that foul deed upon it , the aspect of the spot be peace . no specie of crime have ever be commit in the bad rookery about seven dial that have not be also do in the face of that rustic calm which still , in spite of all , we look on with a tender , half - mournful yearning , and associate with peace .

 CHAPTER VIII . 

 before the storm . 

 so the dinner at Audley Court be postpone , and Miss Alicia have to wait still long for an introduction to the handsome young widower , Mr. George Talboys . 

 I be afraid , if the real truth be to be tell , there be , perhaps , something of affectation in the anxiety this young lady express to make George 's acquaintance ; but if poor Alicia for a moment calculate upon arouse any latent spark of jealousy lurk in her cousin 's breast by this exhibition of interest , she be not so well acquainted with Robert Audley 's disposition as she might have be . indolent , handsome , and indifferent , the young barrister take life as altogether too absurd a mistake for any one event in its foolish course to be for a moment consider seriously by a sensible man . 

 his pretty , gipsy - face cousin might have be over head and ear in love with he ; and she might have tell he so , in some charming , roundabout , womanly fashion , a hundred time a day for all the three hundred and sixty - five day in the year ; but unless she have wait for some privileged 29th of February , and walk straight up to he , say , " Robert , please will you marry I ? " I very much doubt if he would ever have discover the state of her feeling .

 now fish , except to the devoted disciple of Izaak Walton , be not the most lively of occupation ; therefore , it be scarcely , perhaps , to be wonder that on the day after Lady Audley 's departure , the two young man   one of whom be disable by that heart wound which he bear so quietly , from really take pleasure in anything , and the other of whom look upon almost all pleasure as a negative kind of trouble   begin to grow weary of the shade of the willow overhang the wind stream about Audley .

 CHAPTER IX . 

 after the storm .

 CHAPTER X. 

 miss .

 " a disappointment ! " 

 " yes , Mr. Audley , a very cruel one , " answer my lady . " I receive the other morning a telegraphic message from my dear old friend and school - mistress , tell I that she be die , and that if I want to see she again , I must hasten to she immediately . the telegraphic dispatch contain no address , and of course , from that very circumstance , I imagine that she must be live in the house in which I leave she three year ago .  in spite of Lady Audley 's fascination , and in spite of Robert 's very unqualified admiration of she , the barrister could not overcome a vague feeling of uneasiness on this quiet September evening .

 she give a little nervous shudder .

 later in the evening Sir Michael ask for some music , and my lady go to the piano . Robert Audley stroll after she to the instrument to turn over the leaf of her music ; but she play from memory , and he be spar the trouble his gallantry would have impose upon he .

 Robert Audley feel very low - spirited as he walk slowly home between the shadowy meadow ; more low - spirited still when he re - enter the sitting room at Sun Inn , where he and George have lounge together , star out of the window and smoke their cigar .

 with Mr. Audley 's lymphatic nature , determination be so much the exception rather than the rule , that when he do for once in his life resolve upon any course of action , he have a certain dog , iron - like obstinacy that push he on to the fulfillment of his purpose .

 still miss .

 CHAPTER XIII . 

 trouble dreams .

 CHAPTER XIV . 

 PHOEBE 'S SUITOR . 

 " Mr. George Talboys . any person who have meet this gentleman since the 7th inst . , or who possess any information respect he subsequent to that date , will be liberally reward on communicate with A.Z. , 14 Chancery Lane . " 

 Sir Michael Audley read the above advertisement in the second column of the Times , as he sit at breakfast with my lady and Alicia two or three day after Robert 's return to town .
