 CHAPTER XV . 

 on THE WATCH . 

 upon a lower morning late in November , with the yellow fog low upon the flat meadow , and the blinded cattle grope their way through the dim obscurity , and blunder stupidly against black and leafless hedge , or stumble into ditch , undistinguishable in the hazy atmosphere ; with the village church loom brown and dingy through the uncertain light ; with every wind path and cottage door , every gable end and gray old chimney , every village child and straggle cur seem strange and weird of aspect in the semi - darkness , Phoebe Marks and her Cousin Luke make their way through the churchyard of Audley , and present themselves before a shivering curate , whose surplice hang in damp fold , soddene by the morning mist , and whose temper be not improve by his have wait five minute for the bride and bridegroom . 

 Luke Marks , dress in his ill - fitting Sunday clothe , look by no means handsomer than in his every - day apparel ; but Phoebe , array in a rustle silk of delicate gray , that have be wear about half a dozen time by her mistress , look , as the few spectator of the ceremony remark , " quite the lady . " 

 a very dim and shadowy lady , vague of outline , and faint of color , with eye , hair , complexion and dress all melt into such pale and uncertain shade that , in the obscure light of the foggy November morning a superstitious stranger might have mistake the bride for the ghost of some other bride , dead and bury in the vault below the church . 

 Mr. Luke Marks , the hero of the occasion , think very little of all this . he have secure the wife of his choice , and the object of his life - long ambition a public house . my lady have provide the seventy - five pound necessary for the purchase of the good - will and fixture , with the stock of ale and spirit , of a small inn in the center of a lonely little village , perch on the summit of a hill , and call Mount Stanning . it be not a very pretty house to look at ; it have something of a tumble - down , weather - beat appearance , stand , as it do , upon high ground , shelter only by four or five bare and overgrown poplar , that have shoot up too rapidly for their strength , and have a blighted , forlorn look in consequence . the wind have have its own way with the Castle Inn , and have sometimes make cruel use of its power . it be the wind that batter and bend the low , thatched roof of outhouse and stable , till they hang over and lurch forward , as a slouch hat hang over the low forehead of some village ruffian ; it be the wind that shake and rattle the wooden shutter before the narrow casement , till they hang break and dilapidate upon their rusty hinge ; it be the wind that overthrow the pigeon house , and break the vane that have be imprudently set up to tell the movement of its mightiness ; it be the wind that make light of any little bit of wooden trelli - work , or creep plant , or tiny balcony , or any modest decoration whatsoever , and tear and scatter it in its scornful fury ; it be the wind that leave mossy secretion on the discolored surface of the plaster wall ; it be the wind , in short , that shatter , and ruin , and rent , and trample upon the tottering pile of building , and then fly shriek off , to riot and glory in its destroy strength . the dispirit proprietor grow tired of his long struggle with this mighty enemy ; so the wind be leave to work its own will , and the Castle Inn fall slowly to decay . but for all that it suffer without , it be not the less prosperous within door . sturdy drover stop to drink at the little bar ; well - to - do farmer spend their evening and talk politic in the low , wainscoted parlor , while their horse munch some suspicious mixture of moldy hay and tolerable bean in the tumble - down stable . sometimes even the member of the Audley hunt stop to drink and bait their horse at the Castle Inn ; while , on one grand and never - to - be - forget occasion , a dinner have be order by the master of the hound for some thirty gentleman , and the proprietor drive nearly mad by the importance of the demand . 

 so Luke Marks , who be by no mean trouble with an eye for the beautiful , think himself very fortunate in become the landlord of the Castle Inn , Mount Stanning .

 but Christmas bring visitor to the ramble old mansion . a country squire and his fat wife occupy the tapestried chamber ; merry girl scamper up and down the long passage , and young man star out of the latticed window , watch for southerly wind and cloudy sky ; there be not an empty stall in the roomy old stable ; an extempore forge have be set up in the yard for the shoeing of hunter ; yelp dog make the place noisy with their perpetual clamor ; strange servant herd together on the garret story ; and every little casement hide away under some pointed gable , and every dormer window in the quaint old roof , glimmer upon the winter 's night with its separate taper , till , come suddenly upon Audley Court , the benighted stranger , mislead by the light , and noise , and bustle of the place , might have easily fall into young Marlowe 's error , and have mistake the hospitable mansion for a good , old - fashioned inn , such as have fade from this earth since the last mail coach and prance tit take their last melancholy journey to the knacker 's yard .

 " and they be "

 " but you trace he there . his father - in - law have see he . "

Lady Audley , do you ever study the theory of circumstantial evidence ? " 

 " how can you ask a poor little woman about such horrid thing ? " exclaim my lady . 

 " circumstantial evidence , " continue the young man , as if he scarcely hear Lady Audley 's interruption " that wonderful fabric which be build out of straw collect at every point of the compass , and which be yet strong enough to hang a man . upon what infinitesimal trifle may sometimes hang the whole secret of some wicked mystery , inexplicable heretofore to the wise upon the earth ! a scrap of paper , a shred of some tear garment , the button off a coat , a word drop incautiously from the overcautious lip of guilt , the fragment of a letter , the shutting or opening of a door , a shadow on a window - blind , the accuracy of a moment test by one of Benson 's watch a thousand circumstance so slight as to be forget by the criminal , but link of iron in the wonderful chain forge by the science of the detective officer ; and lo ! the gallow be build up ; the solemn bell toll through the dismal gray of the early morning , the drop creak under the guilty foot , and the penalty of crime be pay . " 

 faint shadow of green and crimson fall upon my lady 's face from the paint escutcheon in the mullioned window by which she sit ; but every trace of the natural color of that face have fade out , leave it a ghastly ashen gray . 

 sit quietly in her chair , her head fall back upon the amber damask cushion , and her little hand lie powerless in her lap , Lady Audley have faint away . 

 " the radius grow narrow day by day , " say Robert Audley . " George Talboys never reach Southampton . " 

 CHAPTER XVI . 

 ROBERT AUDLEY get his CONGE .

 CHAPTER XVII . 

 AT the CASTLE INN . 

 the little sitting - room into which Phoebe Marks usher the baronet 's nephew be situate on the ground floor , and only separate by a lath - and - plaster partition from the little bar - parlor occupy by the innkeeper and his wife . 

 it seem as though the wise architect who have superintend the building of the Castle Inn have take especial care that nothing but the frailest and most flimsy material should be use , and that the wind , have a special fancy for this unprotected spot , should have full play for the indulgence of its caprice . 

 to this end pitiful woodwork have be use instead of solid masonry ; rickety ceiling have be prop up by fragile rafter , and beam that threaten on every stormy night to fall upon the head of those beneath they ; door whose specialty be never to be shut , yet always to be bang ; window construct with a peculiar view to let in the draft when they be shut , and keep out the air when they be open . the hand of genius have devise this lonely country inn ; and there be not an inch of woodwork , or trowelful of plaster employ in all the rickety construction that do not offer its own peculiar weak point to every assault of its indefatigable foe .

 the writing in the BOOK .

 CHAPTER XXII . 

 come to a STANDSTILL . 

 Mr. Harcourt Talboys live in a prim , square , red - brick mansion , within a mile of a little village call Grange Heath , in dorsetshire . the prim , square , red - brick mansion stand in the center of prim , square ground , scarcely large enough to be call a park , too large to be call anything else so neither the house nor the ground have any name , and the estate be simply designate Squire Talboys ' . 

 perhaps Mr. Harcourt Talboys be the last person in this world with whom it be possible to associate the homely , hearty , rural old english title of squire . he neither hunt nor farm . he have never wear crimson , pink , or top - boot in his life . a southerly wind and a cloudy sky be matter of supreme indifference to he , so long as they do not in any way interfere with his own prim comfort ; and he only care for the state of the crop inasmuch as it involve the hazard of certain rent which he receive for the farm upon his estate . he be a man of about fifty year of age , tall , straight , bony and angular , with a square , pale face , light gray eye , and scanty dark hair , brush from either ear across a bald crown , and thus impart to his physiognomy some faint resemblance to that of a terri a sharp , uncompromising , hard - head terri a terri not to be take in by the clever dog - stealer who ever distinguish himself in his profession . 

 nobody ever remember get upon what be popularly call the blind side of Harcourt Talboys . he be like his own square - build , northern - fronted , shelterless house . there be no shady nook in his character into which one could creep for shelter from his hard daylight . he be all daylight . he look at everything in the same broad glare of intellectual sunlight , and would see no soften shadow that might alter the sharp outline of cruel fact , subdue they to beauty . I do not know if I express what I mean , when I say that there be no curve in his character that his mind run in straight line , never diverge to the right or the left to round off their pitiless angle . with he right be right , and wrong be wrong . he have never in his merciless , conscientious life admit the idea that circumstance might mitigate the blackness of wrong or weaken the force of right . he have cast off his only son because his only son have disobey he , and he be ready to cast off his only daughter at five minute ' notice for the same reason . 

 if this square - build , hard - head man could be possess of such a weakness as vanity , he be certainly vain of his hardness . he be vain of that inflexible squareness of intellect , which make he the disagreeable creature that he be . he be vain of that unwavere obstinacy which no influence of love or pity have ever be know to bend from its remorseless purpose . he be vain of the negative force of a nature which have never know the weakness of the affection , or the strength which may be bear of that very weakness . 

 if he have regret his son 's marriage , and the breach of his own making , between himself and George , his vanity have be more powerful than his regret , and have enable he to conceal it . indeed , unlikely as it appear at the first glance that such a man as this could have be vain , I have little doubt that vanity be the center from which radiate all the disagreeable line in the character of Mr. Harcourt Talboys . I dare say Junius Brutus be vain , and enjoy the approval of awe - stricken Rome when he order his son off for execution . Harcourt Talboys would have send poor George from his presence between the reversed fasce of the lictor , and grimly relish his own agony . Heaven only know how bitterly this hard man may have feel the separation between himself and his only son , or how much the more terrible the anguish might have be make by that unflinche self - conceit which conceal the torture . 

 " my son do I an unpardonable wrong by marry the daughter of a drunken pauper , " Mr. Talboys would answer to any one who have the temerity to speak to he about George , " and from that hour I have no long a son . I wish he no ill . he be simply dead to I . I be sorry for he , as I be sorry for his mother who die nineteen year ago . if you talk to I of he as you would talk of the dead , I shall be ready to hear you . if you speak of he as you would speak of the living , I must decline to listen . " 

 I believe that Harcourt Talboys hug himself upon the gloomy roman grandeur of this speech , and that he would like to have wear a toga , and wrap himself sternly in its fold , as he turn his back upon poor George 's intercessor . George never in his own person make any effort to soften his father 's verdict . he know his father well enough to know that the case be hopeless . 

 " if I write to he , he will fold my letter with the envelope inside , and indorse it with my name and the date of its arrival , " the young man would say , " and call everybody in the house to witness that it have not move he to one soften recollection or one pitiful thought . he will stick to his resolution to his die day . I dare say , if the truth be know , he be glad that his only son have offend he and give he the opportunity of parade his roman virtue . " 

 George have answer his wife thus when she and her father have urge he to ask assistance from Harcourt Talboys . 

 " no my darling , " he would say , conclusively . " it be very hard , perhaps , to be poor , but we will bear it . we will not   go with pitiful face to the stern father , and ask he to give we food and shelter , only to be refuse in long , johnsonian sentence , and make a classical example for the benefit of the neighborhood . no , my pretty one ; it be easy to starve , but it be difficult to stoop . " 

 perhaps poor Mrs. George do not agree very heartily to the first of these two proposition . she have no great fancy for starving , and she whimper pitifully when the pretty pint bottle of champagne , with Cliquot 's and Moet 's brand upon their cork , be exchange for sixpenny ale , procure by a slipshod attendant from the near beer - shop .  " I think dragoon be always rich , " she use to say , peevishly . "

 CHAPTER XXIV . 

 GEORGE 'S letter . 

 Robert Audley do not return to Southampton , but take a ticket for the first up town train that leave Wareham , and reach Waterloo Bridge an hour or two after dark . the snow , which have be hard and crisp in dorsetshire , be a black and greasy slush in the Waterloo Road , thaw by the flare lamp of the gin - palace and the glare gas in the butcher ' shop .

" what an unspeakable boon what an overpowering blessing ! let any man make a calculation of his existence , subtract the hour in which he have be thoroughly happy really and entirely at his ease , without one arriere pensťe to mar his enjoyment without the most infinitesimal cloud to overshadow the brightness of his horizon . let he do this , and surely he will laugh in utter bitterness of soul when he set down the sum of his felicity , and discover the pitiful smallness of the amount . he will have enjoy himself for a week or ten day in thirty year , perhaps . in thirty year of dull December , and bluster March , and showery April , and dark November weather , there may have be seven or eight glorious August day , through which the sun have blaze in cloudless radiance , and the summer breeze have breathe perpetual balm . how fondly we recollect these solitary day of pleasure , and hope for their recurrence , and try to plan the circumstance that make they bright ; and arrange , and predestinate , and diplomatize with fate for a renewal of the remembered joy . as if any joy could ever be build up out of such and such constituent part ! as if happiness be not essentially accidental a bright and wandering bird , utterly irregular in its migration ; with we one summer 's day , and forever go from we on the next ! look at marriage , for instance , " muse Robert , who be as meditative in the jolting vehicle , for whose occupation he be to pay sixpence a mile , as if he have be rid a mustang on the wild loneliness of the prairie .  we be apt to be angry with this cruel hardness in our life this unflinche regularity in the small wheel and mean mechanism of the human machine , which know no stoppage or cessation , though the mainspring be forever hollow , and the hand point to purposeless figure on a shattered dial . 

 who have not feel , in the first madness of sorrow , an unreasone rage against the mute propriety of chair and table , the stiff squareness of Turkey carpet , the unbending obstinacy of the outward apparatus of existence ? we want to root up gigantic tree in a primeval forest , and to tear their huge branch asunder in our convulsive grasp ; and the utmost that we can do for the relief of our passion be to knock over an easy - chair , or smash a few shilling ' worth of Mr. Copeland 's manufacture . 

 madhouse be large and only too numerous ; yet surely it be strange they be not large , when we think of how many helpless wretch must beat their brain against this hopeless persistency of the orderly outward world , as compare with the storm and tempest , the riot and confusion within when we remember how many mind must tremble upon the narrow boundary between reason and unreason , mad to - day and sane to - morrow , mad yesterday and sane to - day .

" but I be in it , and I can not   get out of it ; so I well submit myself to the brown - eyed girl , and do what she tell I patiently and faithfully . what a wonderful solution to life 's enigma there be in petticoat government ! man might lie in the sunshine , and eat lotus , and fancy it ' always afternoon , ' if his wife would let he ! but she will not , bless her impulsive heart and active mind ! she know well than that . who ever hear of a woman take life as it ought to be take ? instead of support it as an unavoidable nuisance , only redeemable by its brevity , she go through it as if it be a pageant or a procession . she dress for it , and simper and grin , and gesticulate for it . she push her neighbor , and struggle for a good place in the dismal march ; she elbow , and writhe , and trample , and prance to the one end of make the most of the misery . she get up early and sit up late , and be loud , and restless , and noisy , and unpitying . she drag her husband on to the woolsack , or push he into Parliament . she drive he full butt at the dear , lazy machinery of government , and knock and buffet he about the wheel , and crank , and screw , and pulley ; until somebody , for quiet 's sake , make he something that she want he to be make . that be why incompetent man sometimes sit in high place , and interpose their poor , muddle intellect between the thing to be do and the people that can do they , make universal confusion in the helpless innocence of well - place incapacity . the square man in the round hole be push into they by their wife . the Eastern potentate who declare that woman be at the bottom of all mischief , should have go a little far and see why it be so . it be because woman be never lazy . they do not   know what it be to be quiet . they be Semiramides , and cleopatra , and Joans of Arc , Queen Elizabeths , and catharine the Second , and they riot in battle , and murder , and clamor and desperation . if they can not   agitate the universe and play at ball with hemisphere , they will make mountain of warfare and vexation out of domestic molehill , and social storm in household teacup . forbid they to hold forth upon the freedom of nation and the wrong of mankind , and they will quarrel with Mrs. Jones about the shape of a mantle or the character of a small maid - servant . to call they the weak sex be to utter a hideous mockery . they be the strong sex , the noisier , the more persevering , the most self - assertive sex . they want freedom of opinion , variety of occupation , do they ? let they have it . let they be lawyer , doctor , preacher , teacher , soldier , legislator anything they like but let they be quiet if they can . " 

 Mr. Audley push his hand through the thick luxuriance of his straight brown hair , and uplift the dark mass in his despair . 

 " I hate woman , " he think , savagely . " they be bold , brazen , abominable creature , invent for the annoyance and destruction of their superior . look at this business of poor George 's ! it be all woman 's work from one end to the other . he marry a woman , and his father cast he off penniless and professionless . he hear of the woman 's death and he break his heart his good honest , manly heart , worth a million of the treacherous lump of self - interest and mercenary calculation which beat in woman 's breast . he go to a woman 's house and he be never see alive again . and now I find myself drive into a corner by another woman , of whose existence I have never think until this day . and and then , " muse Mr. Audley , rather irrelevantly , " there be Alicia , too ; she be another nuisance . she would like I to marry she I know ; and she will make I do it , I dare say , before she be do with I . but I would much rather not ; though she be a dear , bounce , generous thing , bless her poor little heart . " 

 Robert pay his bill and reward the waiter liberally . the young barrister be very willing to distribute his comfortable little income among the people who serve he , for he carry his indifference to all thing in the universe , even to the matter of pound , shilling and penny . perhaps he be rather exceptional in this , as you may frequently find that the philosopher who call life an empty delusion be pretty sharp in the investment of his money , and recognize the tangible nature of India bond , spanish certificate , and egyptian scrip as contrast with the painful uncertainty of an Ego or a non - ego in metaphysics . 

 the snug room in Figtree Court seem dreary in their orderly quiet to Robert Audley upon this particular evening . he have no inclination for his french novel , though there be a packet of uncut romance , comic and sentimental , order a month before , wait his pleasure upon one of the table . he take his favorite meerschaum and drop into his favorite chair with a sigh . 

 " it be comfortable , but it seem so deuced lonely to - night . if poor George be sit opposite to I , or or even George 's sister she be very like he existence might be a little more endurable . but when a fellow 's live by himself for eight or ten year he begin to be bad company . " 

 he burst out laugh presently as he finish his first pipe . 

 " the idea of my thinking of George 's sister , " he think ; " what a preposterous idiot I be ! " 

 the next day 's post bring he a letter in a firm but feminine hand , which be strange to he . he find the little packet lie on his breakfast - table , beside the warm french roll wrap in a napkin by Mrs. Maloney 's careful but rather dirty hand . he contemplate the envelope for some minute before open it not in any wonder as to his correspondent , for the letter bear the postmark of Grange Heath , and he know that there be only one person who be likely to write to he from that obscure village , but in that lazy dreaminess which be a part of his character . 

 " from Clara Talboys , " he murmur slowly , as he look critically at the clearly - shape letter of his name and address . " yes , from Clara Talboys , most decidedly ; I recognize a feminine resemblance to poor George 's hand ; neater than his , and more decide than his , but very like , very like . " 

 he turn the letter over and examine the seal , which bear his friend 's familiar crest . 

 " I wonder what she say to I ? " he think . " it be a long letter , I dare say ; she be the kind of woman who would write a long letter a letter that will urge I on , drive I forward , wrench I out of myself , I have   no doubt . but that can not   be help so here go ! " 

 he tear open the envelope with a sigh of resignation . it contain nothing but George 's two letter , and a few word write on the flap : " I send the letter ; please preserve and return they C.T. " 

 the letter , write from Liverpool , tell nothing of the writer 's life except his sudden determination of start for a new world , to redeem the fortune that have be ruin in the old . the letter write almost immediately after George 's marriage , contain a full description of his wife such a description as a man could only write within three week of a love match a description in which every feature be minutely catalogue , every grace of form or beauty of expression fondly dwelt upon , every charm of manner lovingly depict . 

 Robert Audley read the letter three time before he lay it down . 

 " if George could have know for what a purpose this description would serve when he write it , " think the young barrister , ' surely his hand would have fall paralyze by horror , and powerless to shape one syllable of these tender word . " 

 CHAPTER XXV . 

 RETROGRADE INVESTIGATION . 

 the dreary London January drag its dull length slowly out . the last slender record of Christmas time be sweep away , and Robert Audley still linger in town still spend his lonely evening in his quiet sitting - room in Figtree Court still wander listlessly in the Temple Gardens on sunny morning , absently listen to the child 's babble , idly watch their play . he have many friend among the inhabitant of the quaint old building round he ; he have other friend far away in pleasant country place , whose spare bedroom be always at Bob 's service , whose cheerful fireside have snugly luxurious chair specially allotted to he . but he seem to have lose all taste for companionship , all sympathy with the pleasure and occupation of his class , since the disappearance of George Talboys . elderly bencher indulge in facetious observation upon the young man 's pale face and moody manner . they suggest the probability of some unhappy attachment , some feminine ill - usage as the secret cause of the change . they tell he to be of good cheer , and invite he to supper - party , at which " lovely woman , with all her fault , God bless she , " be drunk by gentleman who shed tear as they propose the toast , and be maudlin and unhappy in their cup toward the close of the entertainment . Robert have no inclination for the wine - bibbing and the punch - making . the one idea of his life have become his master . he be the bonden slave of one gloomy think one horrible presentiment . a dark cloud be brood above his uncle 's house , and it be his hand which be to give the signal for the thunder - clap , and the tempest that be to ruin that noble life . 

 " if she would only take warning and run away , " he say to himself sometimes . " Heaven know , I have give she a fair chance . why do not   she take it and run away ? " 

 he hear sometimes from Sir Michael , sometimes from Alicia . the young lady 's letter rarely contain more than a few curt line inform he that her papa be well ; and that Lady Audley be in very high spirit , amuse herself in her usual frivolous manner , and with her usual disregard for other people . 

 a letter from Mr. Marchmont , the Southampton schoolmaster , inform Robert that little Georgey be go on very well , but that he be behindhand in his education , and have not yet pass the intellectual Rubicon of word of two syllable . Captain Maldon have call to see his grandson , but that privilege have be withhold from he , in accordance with Mr. Audley 's instruction . the old man have furthermore send a parcel of pastry and sweetmeat to the little boy , which have also be reject on the ground of indigestible and bilious tendency in the edible . 

 toward the close of February , Robert receive a letter from his cousin Alicia , which hurry he one step far forward toward his destiny , by cause he to return to the house from which he have become in a manner exile at the instigation of his uncle 's wife . 

 " Papa be very ill , " Alicia write ; " not dangerously ill , thank God ; but confine to his room by an attack of low fever which have succeed a violent cold . come and see he , Robert , if you have any regard for your near relation . he have speak about you several time ; and I know he will be glad to have you with he . come at once , but say nothing about this letter . 

 " from your affectionate cousin , ALICIA . " 

 a sick and deadly terror chill Robert Audley 's heart , as he read this letter a vague yet hideous fear , which he dare not shape into any definite form . 

 " have I do right ? " he think , in the first agony of this new horror " have I do right to tamper with justice ; and to keep the secret of my doubt in the hope that I be shield those I love from sorrow and disgrace ? what shall I do if I find he ill , very ill , die perhaps , die upon her breast ! what shall I do ? " 

 one course lie clear before he ; and the first step of that course be a rapid journey to Audley Court . he pack his portmanteau , jump into a cab , and reach the railway station within an hour of his receipt of Alicia 's letter , which have come by the afternoon post . 

 the dim village light flicker faintly through the grow dusk when Robert reach Audley . he leave his portmanteau with the station - master , and walk at a leisurely pace through the quiet lane that lead away to the still loneliness of the Court . the over - arching tree stretch their leafless branch above his head , bare and weird in the dusky light . a low moan wind sweep across the flat meadow land , and toss those rugged branch hither and thither against the dark gray sky . they look like the ghostly arm of shrunken and withered giant , beckon Robert to his uncle 's house . they look like threaten phantom in the chill winter twilight , gesticulate to he to hasten upon his journey . the long avenue so bright and pleasant when the perfume lime scatter their light bloom upon the pathway , and the dog - rose leaf float on the summer air , be terribly bleak and desolate in the cheerless interregnum that divide the homely joy of Christmas from the pale blush of come spring a dead pause in the year , in which nature seem to lie in a trance sleep , await the wondrous signal for the budding of the flower . 

 a mournful presentiment creep into Robert Audley 's heart as he draw near to his uncle 's house . every change outline in the landscape be familiar to he ; every bend of the tree ; every caprice of the untrammeled branch ; every undulation in the bare hawthorn hedge , break by dwarf horse - chestnut , stunt willow , blackberry and hazel bush . 

 Sir Michael have be a second father to the young man , a generous and noble friend , a grave and earnest adviser ; and perhaps the strong sentiment of Robert 's heart be his love for the gray - bearded baronet . but the grateful affection be so much a part of himself , that it seldom find an outlet in word , and a stranger would never have fathom the depth of feeling which lay , a deep and powerful current , beneath the stagnant surface of the barrister 's character . 

 " what would become of this place if my uncle be to die ? " he think , and he draw near to the ivied archway , and the still water - pool , coldly gray in the twilight . " would other people live in the old house , and sit under the low oak ceiling in the homely familiar room ? " 

 that wonderful faculty of association , so interweave with the inmost fiber of even the hard nature , fill the young man 's breast with a prophetic pain as he remember that , however long or late , the day must come on which the oaken shutter would be close for awhile , and the sunshine shut out of the house he love . it be painful to he even to remember this ; as it must always be painful to think of the narrow lease the great upon this earth can ever hold of its grandeur . be it so wonderful that some wayfarer drop asleep under the hedge , scarcely care to toil onward on a journey that lead to no abide habitation ? be it wonderful that there have be quietist in the world ever since Christ 's religion be first preach upon earth . be it strange that there be a patient endurance and tranquil resignation , calm expectation of that which be to come on the further shore of the dark flow river ? be it not rather to be wonder that anybody should ever care to be great for greatness ' sake ; for any other reason than pure conscientiousness ; the simple fidelity of the servant who fear to lay his talent by in a napkin , know that indifference be near akin to dishonesty ? if Robert Audley have live in the time of Thomas a Kempis , he would very likely have build himself a narrow hermitage amid some forest loneliness , and spend his life in tranquil imitation of the reputed author of the Imitation . as it be , Figtree Court be a pleasant hermitage in its way , and for breviary and book of Hours , I be ashamed to say the young barrister substitute Paul de Kock and Dumas , fil . but his sin be of so simply negative an order , that it would have be very easy for he to have abandon they for negative virtue .

 the man who open the door to the unlooke - for visitor , brighten as he recognize his master 's nephew .

" my lady and Miss Audley have din early during my master 's illness , but I can bring you anything you would please to take , sir . "

 my lady answer the question .

 the whisper in which he utter these word be too low to reach the other side of the room , where Alicia sit .

"

 " indeed . "

 Mr. Dawson look up with an expression of surprise upon his quiet face .

" for some month past I have struggle with doubt and suspicion which have embitter my life . they have grow strong every day ; and they will not be set at rest by the commonplace sophistry and the shallow argument with which man try to deceive themselves rather than believe that which of all thing upon earth they most fear to believe . I do not think that the woman who bear my uncle 's name , be worthy to be his wife . I may wrong she . Heaven grant that it be so . but if I do , the fatal chain of circumstantial evidence never yet link itself so closely about an innocent person . I wish to set my doubt at rest or or to confirm my fear . there be but one manner in which I can do this . I must trace the life of my uncle 's wife backward , minutely and carefully , from this night to a period of six year ago . this be the twenty - fourth of February , fifty - nine . I want to know every record of her life between to - night and the February of the year fifty - three . "

 " yes , I wish to clear she from a very dreadful suspicion . " 

 " which exist only in your mind ? " 

 " and in the mind of one other person . "

 " from a school at Brompton , a school keep by a lady of the name of Vincent . it be Mrs. Vincent 's strong recommendation that induce I to receive Miss Graham into my family without any more special knowledge of her antecedent . "

 Lucy Audley look up from her occupation among the fragile china cup and watch Robert rather anxiously as he walk softly to his uncle 's room and back again to the boudoir . she look very pretty and innocent , seat behind the graceful group of delicate opal china and glitter silver . surely a pretty woman never look pretty than when make tea . the most feminine and most domestic of all occupation impart a magic harmony to she every movement , a witchery to she every glance . the float mist from the boiling liquid in which she infuse the soothe herb ; whose secret be know to she alone , envelope she in a cloud of scented vapor , through which she seem a social fairy , weave potent spell with Gunpowder and Bohea . at the tea - table she reign omnipotent , unapproachable . what do man know of the mysterious beverage ? read how poor Hazlitt make his tea , and shudder at the dreadful barbarism . how clumsily the wretched creature attempt to assist the witch president of the tea - tray ; how hopelessly they hold the kettle , how continually they imperil the frail cup and saucer , or the taper hand of the priestess . to do away with the tea - table be to rob woman of her legitimate empire . to send a couple of hulk man about among your visitor , distribute a mixture make in the housekeeper 's room , be to reduce the most social and friendly of ceremony to a formal giving out of ration . well the pretty influence of the tea cup and saucer gracefully wield in a woman 's hand than all the inappropriate power snatch at the point of the pen from the unwilling sterner sex . imagine all the woman of England elevate to the high level of masculine intellectuality , superior to crinoline ; above pearl powder and Mrs. Rachael Levison ; above take the pain to be pretty ; above tea - table and that cruelly scandalous and rather satirical gossip which even strong man delight in ; and what a drear , utilitarian , ugly life the sterner sex must lead . 

 my lady be by no mean strong - minded . the starry diamond upon her white finger flash hither and thither among the tea - thing , and she bend her pretty head over the marvelous indian tea - caddy of sandal - wood and silver , with as much earnestness as if life hold no high purpose than the infusion of Bohea .

 he seat himself at the little table and look across it at his Cousin Alicia , who sit with a book in her lap , and have the air of be very much absorb by its page . the bright brunette complexion have lose its glowing crimson , and the animation of the young lady 's manner be suppress on account of her father 's illness , no doubt , Robert think .

 CHAPTER XXVII . 

 begin AT the other END . 

 Robert Audley walk slowly through the leafless grove , under the bare and shadowless tree in the gray February atmosphere , think as he go of the discovery he have just make . 

 " I have that in my pocket - book , " he ponder , " which form the connect link between the woman whose death George Talboys read of in the Times newspaper and the woman who rule in my uncle 's house . the history of Lucy Graham end abruptly on the threshold of Mrs. Vincent 's school . she enter that establishment in August , 1854 . the schoolmistress and her assistant can tell I this but they can not tell I whence she come . they can not give I one clew to the secret of her life from the day of her birth until the day she enter that house .  within an hour of the receipt of this message , Mr. Audley arrive at the King's - cross station , and take his ticket for Wildernsea by an express train that start at a quarter before two . 

 the shriek engine bear he on the dreary northward journey , whirl he over desert waste of flat meadow - land and bare cornfield , faintly tint with fresh sprouting green . this northern road be strange and unfamiliar to the young barrister , and the wide expanse of the wintry landscape chill he by its aspect of bare loneliness . the knowledge of the purpose of his journey blight every object upon which his absent glance fix themselves for a moment , only to wander wearily away ; only to turn inward upon that far dark picture always present itself to his anxious mind . 

 it be dark when the train reach the Hull terminus , but Mr. Audley 's journey be not end . amidst a crowd of porter and scatter heap of that incongruous and heterogeneous luggage with which traveler incumber themselves , he be lead , bewildered and half asleep , to another train which be to convey he along the branch line that sweep past Wildernsea , and skirt the border of the German Ocean . 

 half an hour after leave Hull , Robert feel the briny freshness of the sea upon the breeze that blow in at the open window of the carriage , and an hour afterward the train stop at a melancholy station , build amid a sandy desert , and inhabit by two or three gloomy official , one of whom ring a terrific peal upon a harshly clang bell as the train approach . 

 Mr. Audley be the only passenger who alight at the dismal station . the train sweep on to the gayer scene before the barrister have time to collect his sens , or to pick up the portmanteau which have be discover with some difficulty amid a black cavern of baggage only illuminate by one lantern . 

 " I wonder whether settler in the backwood of America feel as solitary and strange as I feel to - night ? " he think , as he star hopelessly about he in the darkness . 

 he call to one of the official , and point to his portmanteau . 

 " will you carry that to the near hotel for I ? " he ask " that be to say , if I can get a good bed there . " 

 the man laugh as he shoulder the portmanteau . 

 " you can get thirty bed , I dare say , sir , if you want them , " he say . " we be not   over busy at Wildernsea at this time o ' year . this way , sir . " 

 the porter open a wooden door in the station wall , and Robert Audley find himself upon a wide bowling - green of smooth grass , which surround a huge , square building , that loom darkly on he through the winter 's night , its black solidity only relieve by two light window , far apart from each other , and glimmer redly like beacon on the darkness . 

 " this be the Victoria Hotel , sir , " say the porter . " you would not   believe the crowd of company we have down here in the summer . " 

 in the face of the bare grass - plat , the tenantless wooden alcove , and the dark window of the hotel , it be indeed rather difficult to imagine that the place be ever gay with merry people take pleasure in the bright summer weather ; but Robert Audley declare himself willing to believe anything the porter please to tell he , and follow his guide meekly to a little door at the side of the big hotel , which lead into a comfortable bar , where the humbler class of summer visitor be accommodate with such refreshment as they please to pay for , without run the gantlet of the prim , white - waistcoated waiter on guard at the principal entrance . 

 but there be very few attendant retain at the hotel in the bleak February season , and it be the landlord himself who usher Robert into a dreary wilderness of polished mahogany table and horsehair cushion chair , which he call the coffee - room . 

 Mr. Audley seat himself close to the wide steel fender , and stretch his cramped leg upon the hearth - rug , while the landlord drive the poker into the vast pile of coal , and send a ruddy blaze roar upward through the chimney . 

 " if you would prefer a private room , sir " the man begin . 

 " no , thank you , " say Robert , indifferently ; " this room seem quite private enough just now . if you will order I a mutton chop and a pint of sherry , I shall be oblige . " 

 " certainly , sir . " 

 " and I shall be still more obliged if you will favor I with a few minute ' conversation before you do so . " 

 " with very great pleasure , sir , " the landlord answer , good - naturedly . " we see so very little company at this season of the year , that we be only too glad to oblige those gentleman who do visit we . any information which I can afford you respect the neighborhood of Wildernsea and its attraction , " add the landlord , unconsciously quote a small hand - book of the watering - place which he sell in the bar , " I shall be most happy to " 

 " but I do not   want to know anything about the neighborhood of Wildernsea , " interrupt Robert , with a feeble protest against the landlord 's volubility . " I want to ask you a few question about some people who once live here . " 

 the landlord bow and smile , with an air which imply his readiness to recite the biography of all the inhabitant of the little seaport , if require by Mr. Audley to do so . 

 " how many year have you live here ? " Robert ask , take his memorandum book from his pocket . " will it annoy you if I make note of your reply to my question ? " 

 " not at all , sir , " reply the landlord , with a pompous enjoyment of the air of solemnity and importance which pervade this business . " any information which I can afford that be likely to be of ultimate value " 

 " yes , thank you , " Robert murmur , interrupt the flow of word . " you have live here " 

 " six year , sir . " 

 " since the year fifty - three ? " 

 " since November , in the year fifty - two , sir . I be in business at Hull prior to that time . this house be only complete in the October before I enter it . " 

 " do you remember a lieutenant in the navy , on half - pay , I believe , at that time , call Maldon ? " 

 " Captain Maldon , sir ? " 

 " yes , commonly call Captain Maldon . I see you do remember he . " 

 " yes , sir . Captain Maldon be one of our good customer . he use to spend his evening in this very room , though the wall be damp at that time , and we be not   able to paper the place for nearly a twelvemonth afterward . his daughter marry a young officer that come here with his regiment , at Christmas time in fifty - two . they be marry here , sir , and they travel on the continent for six month , and come back here again . but the gentleman run away to Australia , and leave the lady , a week or two after her baby be bear . the business make quite a sensation in Wildernsea , sir , and Mrs. Mrs. I forget the name " 

 " Mrs. Talboys , " suggest Robert . 

 " to be sure , sir , Mrs. Talboys . Mrs. Talboys be very much pity by the Wildernsea folk , sir , I be go to say , for she be very pretty , and have such nice win way that she be a favorite with everybody who know she . " 

 " can you tell I how long Mr. Maldon and his daughter remain at Wildernsea after Mr. Talboys leave they ? " Robert ask . 

 " well no , sir , " answer the landlord , after a few moment ' deliberation . " I can not   say exactly how long it be . I know Mr. Maldon use to sit here in this very parlor , and tell people how badly his daughter have be treat , and how he have be deceive by a young man he would put so much confidence in ; but I can not   say how long it be before he leave Wildernsea . but Mrs. Barkamb could tell you , sir , " add the landlord , briskly . 

 " Mrs. Barkamb . " 

 " yes , Mrs. Barkamb be the person who own no . 17 North Cottages , the house in which Mr. Maldon and his daughter live . she be a nice , civil spoken , motherly woman , sir , and I be sure she will tell you anything you may want to know . " 

 " thank you , I will call upon Mrs. Barkamb to - morrow . stay one more question . should you recognize Mrs. Talboys if you be to see she ? " 

 " certainly , sir . as sure as I should recognize one of my own daughter . " 

 Robert Audley write Mrs. Barkamb 's address in his pocket - book , eat his solitary dinner , drink a couple of glass of sherry , smoke a cigar , and then retire to the apartment in which a fire have be light for his comfort . 

 he soon fall asleep , wear out with the fatigue of hurrying from place to place during the last two day ; but his slumber be not a heavy one , and he hear the disconsolate moaning of the wind upon the sandy waste , and the long wave roll in monotonously upon the flat shore . mingle with these dismal sound , the melancholy thought engender by his joyless journey repeat themselves in never - vary succession in the chaos of his slumber brain , and make themselves into vision of thing that never have be and never could be upon this earth , but which have some vague relation to real event remember by the sleeper . 

 in those troublesome dream he see Audley Court , root up from amidst the green pasture and the shady hedgerow of Essex , stand bare and unprotecte upon that desolate northern shore , threaten by the rapid rise of a boisterous sea , whose wave seem gather upward to descend and crush the house he love . as the hurrying wave roll near and near to the stately mansion , the sleeper see a pale , starry face look out of the silvery foam , and know that it be my lady , transform into a mermaid , beckon his uncle to destruction . beyond that rise sea great mass of cloud , blacker than the black ink , more dense than the dark night , lower upon the dreamer 's eye ; but as he look at the dismal horizon the storm - cloud slowly part , and from a narrow rent in the darkness a ray of light stream out upon the hideous wave , which slowly , very slowly , recede , leave the old mansion safe and firmly rooted on the shore . 

 Robert awake with the memory of this dream in his mind , and a sensation of physical relief , as if some heavy weight , which have oppress he all the night , have be lift from his breast . 

 he fall asleep again , and do not awake until the broad winter sunlight shine upon the window - blind , and the shrill voice of the chambermaid at his door announce that it be half - past eight o'clock . at a quarter - before ten he have leave Victoria Hotel , and be make his way along the lonely platform in front of a row of shadowless house that face the sea . 

 this row of hard , uncompromising , square - build habitation stretch away to the little harbor , in which two or three merchant vessel and a couple of collier be anchor . beyond the harbor there loomed , gray and cold upon the wintry horizon , a dismal barrack , part from the Wildernsea house by a narrow creek , span by an iron drawbridge . the scarlet coat of the sentinel who walk backward and forward between two cannon , place at remote angle before the barrack wall , be the only scrap of color that relieve the neutral - tinted picture of the gray stone house and the leaden sea . 

 on one side of the harbor a long stone pier stretch out far away into the cruel loneliness of the sea , as if build for the especial accommodation of some modern Timon , too misanthropical to be satisfied even with the solitude of Wildernsea , and anxious to get still far away from his fellow - creature . 

 it be on that pier George Talboys have first meet his wife , under the blaze glory of a midsummer sky , and to the music of a bray band . it be there that the young cornet have first yield to that sweet delusion , that fatal infatuation which have exercise so dark an influence upon his after - life . 

 Robert look savagely at this solitary watering - place the shabby seaport . 

 " it be such a place as this , " he think , " that work a strong man 's ruin . he come here , heart whole and happy , with no well experience of woman than be to be learn at a flower - show or in a ball - room ; with no more familiar knowledge of the creature than he have of the far - away satellite or the remoter planet ; with a vague notion that she be a whirling teetotum in pink or blue gauze , or a graceful automaton for the display of milliner ' manufacture . he come to some place of this kind , and the universe be suddenly narrow into about half a dozen acre ; the mighty scheme of creation be crush into a bandbox . the far - away creature whom he have see float about he , beautiful and indistinct , be bring under his very nose ; and before he have time to recover his bewilderment , hey presto , the witchcraft have begin ; the magic circle be draw around he ! the spell be at work , the whole formula of sorcery be in full play , and the victim be as powerless to escape as the marble - legged prince in the eastern story . " 

 ruminate in this wise , Robert Audley reach the house to which he have be direct as the residence of Mrs. Barkamb . he be admit immediately by a prim , elderly servant , who usher he into a sitting - room as prim and elderly - look as herself . Mrs. Barkamb , a comfortable matron of about sixty year of age , be sit in an arm - chair before a bright handful of fire in the shin grate . an elderly terrier , whose black - and - tan coat be thickly sprinkle with gray , repose in Mrs. Barkamb 's lap . every object in the quiet sitting - room have an elderly aspect of simple comfort and precision , which be the evidence of outward repose . 

 " I should like to live here , " Robert think , " and watch the gray sea slowly roll over the gray sand under the still , gray sky . I should like to live here , and tell the bead upon my rosary , and repent and rest . " 

 he seat himself in the arm - chair opposite Mrs. Barkamb , at that lady 's invitation , and place his hat upon the ground . the elderly terri descend from his mistress ' lap to bark at and otherwise take objection to this hat . 

 " you be wish , I suppose , sir , to take one be quiet , Dash one of the cottage , " suggest Mrs. Barkamb , whose mind run in one narrow groove , and whose life during the last twenty year have be an unvarying round of house - letting . 

 Robert Audley explain the purpose of his visit . 

 " I come to ask one simple question , " he say , in conclusion , " I wish to discover the exact date of Mrs. Talboys ' departure from Wildernsea . the proprietor of the Victoria Hotel inform I that you be the most likely person to afford I that information . " 

 Mrs. Barkamb deliberate for some moment . 

 " I can give you the date of Captain Maldon 's departure , " she say , " for he leave no . 17 considerably in my debt , and I have the whole business in black and white ; but with regard to Mrs. Talboys " 

 Mrs. Barkamb pause for a few moment before resume . 

 " you be aware that Mrs. Talboys leave rather abruptly ? " she ask . 

 " I be not aware of that fact . " 

 " indeed ! yes , she leave abruptly , poor little woman ! she try to support herself after her husband 's desertion by give music lesson ; she be a very brilliant pianist , and succeed pretty well , I believe . but I suppose her father take her money from she , and spend it in public house . however that might be , they have a very serious misunderstanding one night ; and the next morning Mrs. Talboys leave Wildernsea , leave she little boy , who be out at nurse in the neighborhood . " 

 " but you can not tell I the date of her leave ? " 

 " I be afraid not , " answer Mrs. Barkamb ; " and yet , stay . Captain Maldon write to I upon the day his daughter leave . he be in very great distress , poor old gentleman , and he always come to I in his trouble . if I could find that letter , it might be date , you know might not   it , now ? " 

 Mr. Audley say that it be only probable the letter be date . 

 Mrs. Barkamb retire to a table in the window on which stand an old - fashioned mahogany desk , line with green baize , and suffer from a plethora of document , which ooze out of it in every direction . letter , receipt , bill , inventory and tax - paper be mingle in hopeless confusion ; and among these Mrs. Barkamb set to work to search for Captain Maldon 's letter . 

 Mr. Audley wait very patiently , watch the gray cloud sail across the gray sky , the gray vessel glide past upon the gray sea . 

 after about ten minute ' search , and a great deal of rustle , crackling , fold and unfolding of the paper , Mrs. Barkamb utter an exclamation of triumph . 

 " I have   get the letter , " she say ; " and there be a note inside it from Mrs. Talboys . " 

 Robert Audley 's pale face flush a vivid crimson as he stretch out his hand to receive the paper . 

 " the person who steal Helen Maldon 's love - letter from George 's trunk in my chamber might have save themselves the trouble , " he think . 

 the letter from the old lieutenant be not long , but almost every other word be underscored . 

 " my generous friend , " the writer begin Mr. Maldon have try the lady 's generosity pretty severely during his residence in her house , rarely pay his rent until threaten with the intrude presence of the broker 's man " I be in the depth of despair . my daughter have leave I ! you may imagine my feeling ! we have a few word last night upon the subject of money matter , which subject have always be a disagreeable one between we , and on rise this morning I find I be desert ! the enclose from Helen be wait for I on the parlor table . 

 " your in distraction and despair , 

 " HENRY MALDON . 

 " NORTH COTTAGES , August 16th , 1854 . " 

 the note from Mrs. Talboys be still more brief . it begin abruptly thus : 

 " I be weary of my life here , and wish , if I can , to find a new one . I go out into the world , dissever from every link which bind I to the hateful past , to seek another home and another fortune . forgive I if I have be fretful , capricious , changeable . you should forgive I , for you know why I have be so . you know the secret which be the key to my life . 

 " HELEN TALBOYS . " 

 these line be write in a hand that Robert Audley know only too well . 

 he sit for a long time ponder silently over the letter write by Helen Talboys . 

 what be the meaning of those two last sentence " you should forgive I , for you know why I have be so . you know the secret which be the key to my life ? " 

 he weary his brain in endeavor to find a clew to the signification of these two sentence . he could remember nothing , nor could he imagine anything that would throw a light upon their meaning . the date of Helen 's departure , accord to Mr. Maldon 's letter , be the 16th of August , 1854 . Miss Tonks have declare that Lucy Graham enter the school at Crescent Villas upon the 17th or 18th of August in the same year . between the departure of Helen Talboys from the Yorkshire watering - place and the arrival of Lucy Graham at the Brompton school , not more than eight - and - forty hour could have elapse . this make a very small link in the chain of circumstantial evidence , perhaps ; but it be a link , nevertheless , and it fit neatly into its place . 

 " do Mr. Maldon hear from his daughter after she have leave Wildernsea ? " Robert ask . 

 " well , I believe he do hear from she , " Mrs. Barkamb answer ; " but I do not   see much of the old gentleman after that August . I be oblige to sell he up in November , poor fellow , for he owe I fifteen month ' rent ; and it be only by sell his poor little bit of furniture that I could get he out of my place . we part very good friend , in spite of my sending in the broker ; and the old gentleman go to London with the child , who be scarcely a twelvemonth old . " 

 Mrs. Barkamb have nothing more to tell , and Robert have no further question to ask . he request permission to retain the two letter write by the lieutenant and his daughter , and leave the house with they in his pocket - book . 

 he walk straight back to the hotel , where he call for a time - table . an express for London leave Wildernsea at a quarter past one . Robert send his portmanteau to the station , pay his bill , and walk up and down the stone terrace front the sea , wait for the starting of the train . 

 " I have trace the history of Lucy Graham and Helen Talboys to a vanishing point , " he think ; " my next business be to discover the history of the woman who lie bury in Ventnor churchyard . " 

 CHAPTER XXVIII . 

 HIDDEN in the GRAVE . 

 upon his return from Wildernsea , Robert Audley find a letter from his Cousin Alicia , await he at his chamber . 

 " Papa be much well , " the young lady write , " and be very anxious to have you at the Court . for some inexplicable reason , my stepmother have take it into her head that your presence be extremely desirable , and worry I with her frivolous question about your movement . so pray come without delay , and set these people at rest . your affectionate cousin , A.A. " 

 " so my lady be anxious to know my movement , " think Robert Audley , as he sit brood and smoking by his lonely fireside . " she be anxious ; and she question her step - daughter in that pretty , childlike manner which have such a bewitching air of innocent frivolity . poor little creature ; poor unhappy little golden - haired sinner ; the battle between we seem terribly unfair . why do not   she run away while there be still time ? I have give her fair warning , I have show she my card , and work openly enough in this business , Heaven know . why do not   she run away ? " 

 he repeat this question again and again as he fill and empty his meerschaum , surround himself with the blue vapor from his pipe until he look like some modern magician seat in his laboratory . 

 " why do not   she run away ? I would bring no needless shame upon that house , of all other house upon this wide earth . I would only do my duty to my miss friend , and to that brave and generous man who have pledge his faith to a worthless woman . Heaven know I have no wish to punish . Heaven know I be never bear to be the avenger of guilt or the persecutor of the guilty . I only wish to do my duty . I will give she one more warning , a full and fair one , and then " 

 his thought wander away to that gloomy prospect in which he see no gleam of brightness to relieve the dull , black obscurity that encompass the future , shut in his pathway on every side , and spread a dense curtain around and about he , which Hope be powerless to penetrate . he be forever haunt by the vision of his uncle 's anguish , forever torture by the thought of that ruin and desolation which , be bring about by his instrumentality , would seem in a manner his handiwork . but amid all , and through all , Clara Talboys , with an imperious gesture , beckon he onward to her brother 's unknown grave . 

 " shall I go down to Southampton , " he think , " and endeavor to discover the history of the woman who die at Ventnor ? shall I work underground , bribe the paltry assistant in that foul conspiracy , until I find my way to the thrice guilty principal ? no ! not till I have try other mean of discover the truth . shall I go to that miserable old man , and charge he with his share in the shameful trick which I believe to have be play upon my poor friend ? no ; I will not torture that terror - stricken wretch as I torture he a few week ago . I will go straight to that arch - conspirator , and will tear away the beautiful veil under which she hide her wickedness , and will wring from she the secret of my friend 's fate , and banish she forever from the house which her presence have pollute . " 

 he start early the next morning for Essex , and reach Audley before eleven o'clock . 

 early as it be , my lady be out . she have drive to Chelmsford upon a shopping expedition with her step - daughter . she have several call to make in the neighborhood of the town , and be not likely to return until dinner - time . Sir Michael 's health be very much improve , and he would come down stair in the afternoon . would Mr. Audley go to his uncle 's room ? 

 no ; Robert have no wish to meet that generous kinsman . what could he say to he ? how could he smooth the way to the trouble that be to come ? how soften the cruel blow of the great grief that be prepare for that noble and trust heart ? 

 " if I could forgive she the wrong do to my friend , " Robert think , " I should still abhor she for the misery her guilt must bring upon the man who have believe in she . " 

 he tell his uncle 's servant that he would stroll into the village , and return before dinner . he walk slowly away from the Court , wander across the meadow between his uncle 's house and the village , purposeless and indifferent , with the great trouble and perplexity of his life stamp upon his face and reflect in his manner . 

 " I will go into the churchyard , " he think , " and stare at the tombstone . there be nothing I can do that will make I more gloomy than I be . " 

 he be in those very meadow through which he have hurry from Audley Court to the station upon the September day in which George Talboys have disappear . he look at the pathway by which he have go upon that day , and remember his unaccustomed hurry , and the vague feeling of terror which have take possession of he immediately upon lose sight of his friend . 

 " why do that unaccountable terror seize upon I , " he think . " why be it that I see some strange mystery in my friend 's disappearance ? be it a monition , or a monomania ? what if I be wrong after all ? what if this chain of evidence which I have construct link by link , be weave out of my own folly ? what if this edifice of horror and suspicion be a mere collection of crotchet the nervous fancy of a hypochondriacal bachelor ? Mr. Harcourt Talboys see no meaning in the event out of which I have make myself a horrible mystery . I lie the separate link of the chain before he , and he can not recognize their fitness . he be unable to put they together . oh , my God , if it should be in myself all this time that the misery lie ; if " he smile bitterly , and shake his head . " I have the handwriting in my pocket - book which be the evidence of the conspiracy , " he think . " it remain for I to discover the dark half of my lady 's secret . " 

 he avoid the village , still keep to the meadow . the church lie a little way back from the straggling High street , and a rough wooden gate open from the churchyard into a broad meadow , that be border by a running stream , and slop down into a grassy valley dot by group of cattle . 

 Robert slowly ascend the narrow hillside pathway lead up to the gate in the churchyard . the quiet dullness of the lonely landscape harmonize with his own gloom . the solitary figure of an old man hobble toward a stile at the further end of the wide meadow be the only human creature visible upon the area over which the young barrister look . the smoke slowly ascend from the scatter house in the long high street be the only evidence of human life . the slow progress of the hand of the old clock in the church steeple be the only token by which a traveler could perceive that a sluggish course of rustic life have not come to a full stop in the village of Audley . 

 yes , there be one other sign . as Robert open the gate of the churchyard , and stroll listessly into the little inclosure , he become aware of the solemn music of an organ , audible through a half - open window in the steeple . 

 he stop and listen to the slow harmony of a dreamy melody that sound like an extempore composition of an accomplished player . 

 " who would have believe that Audley church could boast such an organ ? " think Robert . " when last I be here , the national schoolmaster use to accompany his child by a primitive performance of common chord . I do not   think the old organ have such music in it . " 

 he linger at the gate , not care to break the lazy spell weave about he by the monotonous melancholy of the organist 's performance . the tone of the instrument , now swell to their full power , now sink to a low , whispering softness , float toward he upon the misty winter atmosphere , and have a soothing influence , that seem to comfort he in his trouble . 

 he close the gate softly , and cross the little patch of gravel before the door of the church . the door have be leave ajar by the organist , perhaps . Robert Audley push it open , and walk into the square porch , from which a flight of narrow stone step wound upward to the organ - loft and the belfry . Mr. Audley take off his hat , and open the door between the porch and the body of the church . he step softly into the holy edifice , which have a damp , moldy smell upon week - day . he walk down the narrow aisle to the altar - rail , and from that point of observation take a survey of the church . the little gallery be exactly opposite to he , but the scanty green curtain before the organ be closely draw , and he could not get a glimpse of the player . 

 the music still roll on . the organist have wander into a melody of Mendelssohn 's , a strain whose dreamy sadness go straight to Robert 's heart . he loiter in the nook and corner of the church , examine the dilapidated memorial of the well - nigh forget dead , and listen to the music . 

 " if my poor friend , George Talboys , have die in my arm , and I have bury he in this quiet church , in one corner of the vault over which I tread to - day , how much anguish of mind , vacillation and torment I might have escape , " think Robert Audley , as he read the fade inscription upon tablet of discolor marble ; " I should have know his fate I should have know his fate ! ah , how much there would have be in that . it be this miserable uncertainty , this horrible suspicion which have poison my very life . " 

 he look at his watch . 

 " half - past one , " he mutter . " I shall have to wait four or five dreary hour before my lady come home from her morning call she pretty visit of ceremony or friendliness . Good Heaven ! what an actress this woman be . what an arch trickster what an all - accomplished deceiver . but she shall play her pretty comedy no long under my uncle 's roof . I have diplomatize long enough . she have refuse to accept an indirect warning . to - night I will speak plainly . " 

 the music of the organ cease , and Robert hear the closing of the instrument . 

 " I will have a look at this new organist , " he think , " who can afford to bury his talent at Audley , and play Mendelssohn 's fine fugue for a stipend of sixteen pound a year . " he linger in the porch , wait for the organist to descend the awkward little stair - case . in the weary trouble of his mind , and with the prospect of get through the five hour in the good way he could , Mr. Audley be glad to cultivate any diversion of thought , however idle . he therefore freely indulge his curiosity about the new organist . 

 the first person who appear upon the steep stone step be a boy in corduroy trouser and a dark linen smock - frock , who shamble down the stair with a good deal of unnecessary clatter of his hobnailed shoe , and who be red in the face from the exertion of blow the bellow of the old organ . close behind this boy come a young lady , very plainly dressed in a black silk gown and a large gray shawl , who start and turn pale at sight of Mr. Audley . 

 this young lady be Clara Talboys . 

 of all people in the world she be the last whom Robert either expect or wish to see . she have tell he that she be go to pay a visit to some friend who live in Essex ; but the county be a wide one , and the village of Audley one of the most obscure and least frequent spot in the whole of its extent . that the sister of his lost friend should be here here where she could watch his every action , and from those action deduce the secret working of his mind , trace his doubt home to their object , make a complication of his difficulty that he could never have anticipate . it bring he back to that consciousness of his own helplessness , in which he have exclaim : 

 " a hand that be strong than my own be beckon I onward on the dark road that lead to my lose friend 's unknown grave . " 

 Clara Talboys be the first to speak . 

 " you be surprised to see I here , Mr. Audley , " she say . 

 " very much surprised . " 

 " I tell you that I be come to Essex . I leave home day before yesterday . I be leave home when I receive your telegraphic message . the friend with whom I be stay be Mrs. Martyn , the wife of the new rector of Mount Stanning . I come down this morning to see the village and church , and as Mrs. Martyn have to pay a visit to the school with the curate and his wife , I stop here and amuse myself by try the old organ . I be not aware till I come here that there be a village call Audley . the place take its name from your family , I suppose ? " 

 " I believe so , " Robert answer , wonder at the lady 's calmness , in contradistinction to his own embarrassment . " I have a vague recollection of hear the story of some ancestor who be call Audley of Audley in the reign of Edward the Fourth . the tomb inside the rail near the altar belong to one of the knight of Audley , but I have never take the trouble to remember his achievement . be you go to wait here for your friend , Miss Talboys ? " 

 " yes ; they be to return here for I after they have finish their round . " 

 " and you go back to Mount Stanning with they this afternoon ? " 

 " yes . " 

 Robert stand with his hat in his hand , look absently out at the tombstone and the low wall of the church yard . Clara Talboys watch his pale face , haggard under the deepening shadow that have rest upon it so long . 

 " you have be ill since I see you last , Mr. Audley , " she say , in a low voice , that have the same melodious sadness as the note of the old organ under her touch . 

 " no , I have not be ill ; I have be only harass , weary by a hundred doubt and perplexity . " 

 he be think as he speak to she : 

 " how much do she guess ? how much do she suspect ? " 

 he have tell the story of George 's disappearance and of his own suspicion , suppress only the name of those concern in the mystery ; but what if this girl should fathom this slender disguise , and discover for herself that which he have choose to withhold .

 CHAPTER XXXI . 

 PHOEBE 'S PETITION . 

 the division between Lady Audley and her step - daughter have not become any narrower in the two month which have elapse since the pleasant Christmas holiday time have be keep at Audley Court . there be no open warfare between the two woman ; there be only an armed neutrality , break every now and then by brief feminine skirmish and transient wordy tempest . I be sorry to say that Alicia would very much have prefer a hearty pitch battle to this silent and undemonstrative disunion ; but it be not very easy to quarrel with my lady . she have soft answer for the turning away of wrath . she could smile bewitchingly at her step - daughter 's open petulance , and laugh merrily at the young lady 's ill - temper . perhaps have she be less amiable , have she be more like Alicia in disposition , the two lady might have expend their enmity in one tremendous quarrel , and might ever afterward have be affectionate and friendly . but Lucy Audley would not make war . she carry forward the sum of her dislike , and put it out at a steady rate of interest , until the breach between her step - daughter and herself , widen a little every day , become a great gulf , utterly impassable by olive - branch - bear dove from either side of the abyss . there can be no reconciliation where there be no open warfare . there must be a battle , a brave , boisterous battle , with pennant wave and cannon roaring , before there can be peaceful treaty and enthusiastic shaking of hand . perhaps the union between France and England owe its great force to the recollection of Cressy and Waterloo , Navarino and Trafalgar . we have hat each other and lick each other and have it out , as the common phrase go ; and we can afford now to fall into each other ' arm and vow eternal friendship and everlaste brotherhood . let we hope that when Northern Yankeedom have decimate and be decimate , bluster Jonathan may fle himself upon his southern brother 's breast , forgiving and forgive . 

 Alicia Audley and her father 's pretty wife have plenty of room for the comfortable indulgence of their dislike in the spacious old mansion . my lady have her own apartment , as we know luxurious chamber , in which all conceivable elegancie have be gather for the comfort of their occupant . Alicia have her own room in another part of the large house . she have her favorite mare , her Newfoundland dog , and her draw material , and she make herself tolerably happy . she be not very happy , this frank , generous - hearted girl , for it be scarcely possible that she could be altogether at ease in the constrained atmosphere of the Court . her father be change ; that dear father over whom she have once reign supreme with the boundless authority of a spoiled child , have accept another ruler and submit to a new dynasty . little by little my lady 's petty power make itself feel in that narrow household ; and Alicia see her father gradually lure across the gulf that divide Lady Audley from her step - daughter , until he stand at last quite upon the other side of the abyss , and look coldly upon his only child across that widen chasm . 


 Alicia feel that he be lose to she . my lady 's beaming smile , my lady 's win word , my lady 's radiant glance and bewitch grace have do their work of enchantment , and Sir Michael have grow to look upon his daughter as a somewhat wilful and capricious young person who have behave with determined unkindness to the wife he love .

 CHAPTER XXXII . 

 the red light in the SKY .

 CHAPTER XXXIII . 

 the bearer of the tiding . 

 it be very late the next morning when Lady Audley emerge from her dressing - room , exquisitely dress in a morning costume of delicate muslin , delicate lace , and embroidery ; but with a very pale face , and with half - circle of purple shadow under her eye . she account for this pale face and these hollow eye by declare that she have sit up read until a very late hour on the previous night .

 the March morning be bleak and dull , and a drizzling rain fall incessantly , obscure the landscape and blot out the distance . there be very few letter by the morning post ; the daily newspaper do not arrive until noon ; and such aid to conversation be miss , there be very little talk at the breakfast table . 

 Alicia look out at the drizzle rain drift against the broad window - pane . 

 " no riding to - day , " she say ; " and no chance of any caller to enliven we , unless that ridiculous Bob come crawl through the wet from Mount Stanning . " 

 have you ever hear anybody , whom you know to be dead , allude to in a light , easy go manner by another person who do not know of his death allude to as do that or this , as perform some trivial everyday operation when you know that he have vanish away from the face of this earth , and separate himself forever from all live creature and their commonplace pursuit in the awful solemnity of death ? such a chance allusion , insignificant though it may be , be apt to send a strange thrill of pain through the mind . the ignorant remark jar discordantly upon the hyper - sensitive brain ; the King of Terrors be desecrate by that unwitte disrespect . Jones , who be wildly enamored of Miss Brown , and who lie awake at night until he loathe his comfortable pillow and tumble his sheet into two twisted rag of linen in his agony , as if he be a prisoner and want to wind they into impromptu rope ;  CHAPTER XXXIV . 

 my LADY tell the truth .

 " no . "

 " shall I tell you ? "

 " my Lady Audley , " cry Robert , suddenly , " you be the incendiary . it be you whose murderous hand kindle those flame . it be you who think by that thrice - horrible deed to rid yourself of I , your enemy and denouncer . what be it to you that other life might be sacrifice ? if by a second massacre of Saint Bartholomew you could have rid yourself of I you would have sacrifice an army of victim . the day be past for tenderness and mercy . for you I can no long know pity or compunction . so far as by spar your shame I can spare other who must suffer by your shame , I will be merciful , but no far . if there be any secret tribunal before which you might be make to answer for your crime , I would have little scruple in be your accuser , but I would spare that generous and high - bear gentleman upon whose noble name your infamy would be reflect .
